## 

## AFF---South Korea

### NUQ---Alliance Low 

#### Trump's policies kill cooperation. 

**Slingbaum '25** \[9dl; Hunter Slingbaum; GWIKS North Korea Program
Certificate of Completion, Research & Development Assistant; 5-8-2025;
"9DASHLINE---The rough road ahead for US-South Korea relations";
9DASHLINE;
https://www.9dashline.com/article/the-rough-road-ahead-for-us-south-korea-relations-1;
Accessed 7-14-2025; TS\]

These [first several months]{.underline} of 2025 [have been especially
**tumultuous** for both the U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates
[and So]{.underline}uth [Ko]{.underline}rea. US President Donald
[Trump]{.underline} has spent the beginning of his second term
[upend]{.underline}ing [longstanding policies and norms]{.underline}.
One of his more recent, all-consuming focuses has been trade deficits
and so-called "[unfair **tariff disparities**]{.underline}", which he
addressed with sweeping "reciprocal" tariffs against nearly every other
nation. One week later, and most of those tariffs were temporarily
paused. His inconsistency and capriciousness have caused US allies to
doubt the reliability of US security guarantees and extended deterrence,
while also stoking concern for the world economy and free trade norms.

South Korea has also seen its fair share of internal turmoil, beginning
with (now former) President Yoon Suk Yeol's martial law declaration in
December 2024. For the last four months, South Korean politics have been
in disarray; the country is still without an elected leader. While the
period of martial law itself was a flash in the pan, the fallout and
existing fractures it revealed in South Korean society and politics are
not easily salved.

Amid this uncertainty, [US-ROK relations]{.underline} were sure to be
tested. But Trump has given South Korean leaders additional reasons to
be concerned. Experts fear that current interim leadership in Seoul may
be pressured [into]{.underline} sweeping concessions with [long-term
consequences]{.underline}.

In the coming months, South Korea should remain focused on its own
presidential election and repairing its domestic fabric, but interim
leadership can also explore ways to weather Trump's demands, including
by reframing existing efforts, as Canada did with their border plan.

Though South Korea has largely not been at the forefront of President
Trump's agenda, that is not to say that the US-ROK relationship has
remained stagnant or purely speculative in the first stretch of the new
administration. To best understand where we are now, it is crucial to
examine three areas: the impacts of South Korean domestic turbulence,
Trump's global trade war, and the prospects of nuclear proliferation.

South Korea's leadership crisis

South Korea's current domestic instability and leadership turnover
threatens to undermine its negotiating position with the US. In both his
first term, and the start of his second, President Trump has fixated on
the interpersonal side of US foreign relations, favouring strong leaders
that have a firm handle on their constituents and are vocal in their
praise of his leadership. Look no further than Japan's former Prime
Minister Shinzo Abe, who leveraged an active personal relationship with
Trump in his first term to gain a number of symbolic and policy wins,
including Trump's adoption of Abe's Free and Open Indo-Pacific (FOIP)
strategy. Former South Korean President Yoon knew this well, privately
and publicly congratulating then President-elect Trump and, soon after,
returning to the golf course to practice his swing for future meetings.

With Yoon officially removed from office by the South Korean
Constitutional Court, his successor will face the challenging task of
building a relationship with Trump from scratch while attempting to
stabilise the domestic political situation. The martial law declaration
may have been the initiating event, but South Korea's 'leadership
crisis' has persisted in the ensuing four months, with the role of
president changing hands three times. The first brought then Prime
Minister Han Duck Soo into a short-lived stint as interim President.
Barely two weeks later, he also faced impeachment and was replaced by
his deputy, Choi Sang Mok. The latest turnover, Han's reinstatement in
March, will not endure either, as Yoon's removal triggered preparations
for the next presidential election, set to be held on 3 June.

This **[instability]{.underline}** is not happenstance; it [is a symptom
of the **polarisation**]{.underline} that has [infected South Korean
politics and threatens the functioning of its democrac]{.underline}y.
The sheer number of both pro- and anti-Yoon protests since the martial
law declaration serve as evidence of this deep divide. According to the
ACLED, January 2025 saw a 61 per cent increase in pro-Yoon
demonstrations compared to December 2024, and such protests have more
closely matched rates of anti-Yoon protests since the start of February
2025. That is all to say, even when a new president is elected in the
coming months, they will have a challenging future ahead in quelling
domestic unrest, unifying a divided country, and presenting a strong
image in future negotiations with the Trump administration.

### NUQ---Alliance Low---Trump 

#### Trump is destroying the alliance

**Berkofsky '25** \[Axel; June 25; Co-head of Asia center; Italian
Institute for International Political Studies, "Troops, Tariffs, and
Tension: The Future of the US--Korea Alliance,"
https://www.ispionline.it/en/publication/troops-tariffs-and-tension-the-future-of-the-us-korea-alliance-212184;
accessed: 7.16.25\]

Donald [Trump wants more **money**]{.underline}. The more the better,
and this time [from South Korea]{.underline}, as it turns out. [On his
campaign trail, Trump announced]{.underline} his entirely unrealistic
[idea to increase South Korea's **annual contribution** to the
stationing of 28,500 US military troops on South Korean soil from 1 to
**10 billion** USD]{.underline}. Evidently, [that is a **non-starter**
request]{.underline}, but fortunately, if there is one thing we have
learned from Donald Trump over the lasts 6 month, it is that -- more
often than not -- much of what he says on Monday is no longer relevant
on Tuesday. For the time being, Washington will reportedly continue
sticking to the bilateral stationing costs agreement adopted in October
2024.

Back then, the US and South Korea agreed on a new five-year plan on
sharing the cost of financing US troops stationed in South Korea. For
the year 2025, it was stipulated, Seoul will increase its annual
financial contributions by 8.3% totalling roughly \$1.13 billion. After
that (i.e. for the four remaining years of the agreement), the increase
of Seoul's contributions will amount to 5% annually. Of course, it was
no accident that Seoul decided to adopt such agreement when the return
of Donald Trump to the US presidency began to look increasingly likely.
Indeed, given that the agreement was adopted between Seoul and the
previous US administration, it can be anything but excluded that [Trump
might rather sooner than later return to the topic and resume **urging**
Seoul to "**pay up**", as he typically puts it]{.underline}.

When announcing to impose tariffs onto all countries except Russia a few
months ago, Trump had indicated that he planned to make South Korean
burden-sharing contributions a part of negotiations over tariffs.
Nonetheless, South Korea is not alone in finding itself being confronted
with [Trump's idea to link **tariffs** with contributions to the
**stationing costs** of US troops in the region]{.underline}. Japan,
hosting 55,000 US military troops on Japanese territory, is on the same
Trumpian blacklist. Seoul has tentatively decided that it is not
planning to allow linking tariffs with its financial contributions to
the stationing costs of US military when negotiating with Washington.
Tokyo has done the same, and many in Japan even suggest that the country
should use its financial power as leverage over Washington. Japan is
holding \$1.13 trillion (roughly half the size of the Italian economy)
in US Treasury bonds and there have been voices in Japan which suggest
that Tokyo could -- if push came to shove -- use the Treasury bonds as
leverage, threatening to sell them on a large scale as part of
negotiation tactics. In fairness, none of said suggestions gained any
traction, but they might still have served as a reminder that the
world's third economy is less of a "pushover alliance partner" than
Trump thinks it is.

[Trump's propensity to **rock** the boat of Washington's **alliances**
in Asia has **no** apparent **limits**]{.underline}, thus far. In late
May, the Wall Street Journal reported that Washington is [considering to
withdraw **4,500 US troops** from South Korea and relocate them to
**Guam**]{.underline}. While Washington and Seoul were quick to deny
that a US troop withdrawal had ever been discussed, Korea expert Victor
Cha writes in an article for the Center for Strategic and International
Studies (CSIS) that the Pentagon, the US Forces in Korea, and the US
Indo-Pacific Command [are indeed seriously considering to reduce the
number of US troops stationed in South Korea]{.underline}. However,
Victor Cha claims to be concerned about further matters, fearing in a
recent Foreign Affairs article that the US [president might become
tempted to revive his attempts to **engage** **Kim** Jong Un. Trump's
"America First" policies]{.underline}, Cha argues, could [push the US
President towards cutting a deal with the North Korean dictator that
could envision Pyongyang agreeing to **arms control** in return for
Trump giving up on the long-established US policy of **denuclearizing**
North Korea and officially recognizing the country as nuclear weapons
state]{.underline}. "Arms control", Cha expounds, implicates convincing
Pyongyang to forego providing weapons technology to Middle Eastern
states in general and Iran in particular. This is an agreement which,
according to Cha, would further [endanger the **security** of
Washington's allies in the region, such as **South Korea** and
Japan]{.underline}.

### NUQ---Alliance Low---Tariffs 

#### Tariffs give Lee an [ultimatum]{.underline}. 

**Min-Hee \'25** \[Kim Min-Hee; PhD in Social Work and Sociology from
the University of Michigan; 7-9-2025; \"For South Korea's New President,
Trump's Tariffs Are an Ultimatum\"; Carnegie Endowment for International
Peace;
https://carnegieendowment.org/emissary/2025/07/south-korea-trump-tariff-economy-lee?lang=en;
Accessed 7-16-2025; TS\]

When he assumed office in June, South Korean President Lee Jae Myung
inherited a country in crisis. His first priority was restoring
political legitimacy to the office of the president following his
predecessor's impeachment for declaring martial law in December 2024.
Now, his political credibility depends on an ambitious economic
reorientation, including addressing youth unemployment and declining
industrial output, among other factors, to reverse South Korea's sagging
economic outlook.

A month later, U.S. President Donald Trump announced [25 percent tariffs
on South Korean]{.underline} (and Japanese) goods. [Trump's **punitive**
trade measures---framed as an "invitation" for South Korea to
"participate in the extraordinary Economy of the United
States]{.underline}"---now [threaten]{.underline} to derail the
[export-dependent recovery]{.underline} that has given Lee's presidency
its early momentum.

Now, Lee's core challenge isn't whether his reforms are well-designed,
but whether he can overcome the external constraints---from global trade
wars to China-U.S. technology competition to global supply chain
disruption---that determine South Korea's economic fate, regardless of
Seoul's policy choices. Lee's pragmatic promises have sparked cautious
optimism, but his ability to deliver depends less on political skill
than on geopolitical developments, corporate governance battles with
interests of the entrenched chaebol (South Korean multinational
conglomerates), and market forces that operate far outside the
president's influence.

Trump's Economic Ultimatum

[Trump's tariff]{.underline} announcement reveals how dramatically U.S.
relations [with]{.underline} its key [Asian allies]{.underline} [have
shifted]{.underline} from strategic partnerships to something akin [to
**economic coercion**]{.underline}. The official tariff letters [to
South Korea]{.underline} and Japan are virtually identical, with only
country names and leader titles changed, signaling that Trump may view
both relationships through the same transactional lens. The ultimatum is
stark: Both countries face 25 percent tariffs on all exports unless
companies relocate manufacturing to the United States by August 1. Trump
characterizes their trade surpluses as threats to the U.S. economy and
national security, transforming routine commercial relations into a
zero-sum security competition.

Although Japan and South Korea face the same ultimatum,
[So]{.underline}uth [Ko]{.underline}rea['s]{.underline}
[**vulnerabilities** run deeper]{.underline}. The country's economy is
significantly more trade-dependent, with exports representing over 40
percent of GDP, compared to Japan's 20 percent. Japan possesses a larger
domestic market and lower reliance on trade as a share of economic
output, providing more cushion against external shocks. In other words,
[So]{.underline}uth [Ko]{.underline}rea['s]{.underline} smaller domestic
market means export [disrupt]{.underline}ion to the [U]{.underline}nited
[S]{.underline}tates hits harder and faster, making Lee's challenge more
acute than Japanese Prime Minister Shigeru Ishiba's in navigating
Trump's demands while maintaining economic growth.

[Trump's announcement]{.underline} [represents]{.underline} an
existential [threat to Lee's]{.underline} governing
[strategy]{.underline}. Lee had staked his candidacy on leveraging South
Korea's strategic value to achieve economic transformation through
enhanced [U.S.--South Korea cooperation]{.underline}. Trump's tariffs
not only [undermine that narrative]{.underline} but [force
Lee]{.underline} [into]{.underline} [an **impossible
choice**]{.underline}: accept economic concessions that could [devastate
South Korean exporters or risk trade confrontation]{.underline} that
would destroy the market rally that fuels his political support.

#### Tariffs [destroy]{.underline} South Korean Companies. 

**Min-Hee \'25** \[Kim Min-Hee; PhD in Social Work and Sociology from
the University of Michigan; 7-9-2025; \"For South Korea's New President,
Trump's Tariffs Are an Ultimatum\"; Carnegie Endowment for International
Peace;
https://carnegieendowment.org/emissary/2025/07/south-korea-trump-tariff-economy-lee?lang=en;
Accessed 7-16-2025; TS\]

The Geopolitical Vise

Beyond [Trump's]{.underline} immediate [tariff **threat**]{.underline},
Lee must navigate U.S.-China technology competition that [forces South
Korean companies into **impossible** choices]{.underline}. Samsung and
SK Hynix face restrictions on Chinese operations even as they invest
billions in American facilities--- including Samsung's impressive \$45
billion investment near Austin, Texas, for semiconductor fabrication
lines and research facilities. This market split [reduces the
flexibility that enabled South Korea's industrial **juggernauts** to
thrive and forces strategic decisions that compromise global market
access regardless of Seoul's preferences]{.underline}.

The technology competition illustrates how great power rivalry
constrains middle power options. South Korean companies built global
leadership by serving both U.S. and Chinese markets, but Washington's
security concerns over the past decade---spanning several
administrations---have required choosing sides in ways that reduce South
Korean competitiveness. Lee's promises of enhanced U.S. cooperation and
domestic innovation sound reasonable in theory, but implementation
timelines may not match the market expectations his campaign rhetoric
created.

The broader trend toward [economic nationalism]{.underline} in major
markets [limits So]{.underline}uth [Ko]{.underline}rea['s]{.underline}
traditional export-led growth [model]{.underline}. While Lee's proposed
pragmatic approach to alliance management may help in bilateral
negotiations, the fundamental shift toward geoeconomic competition
operates beyond any South Korean president's influence and constrains
the policy space available for domestic economic transformation.

### NUQ---Alliance Low---Impossible 

#### It's [impossible]{.underline} for Korea to align with US policies. 

**Min-Hee \'25** \[Kim Min-Hee; PhD in Social Work and Sociology from
the University of Michigan; 7-9-2025; \"For South Korea's New President,
Trump's Tariffs Are an Ultimatum\"; Carnegie Endowment for International
Peace;
https://carnegieendowment.org/emissary/2025/07/south-korea-trump-tariff-economy-lee?lang=en;
Accessed 7-16-2025; TS\]

For Washington, Lee's economic pragmatism creates opportunities for
deeper bilateral cooperation. But [U.S. policymakers]{.underline} should
recognize that South Korea's domestic challenges may [limit Seoul's
ability to fully align with Washington's priorities]{.underline} when
[they conflict with immediate economic **survival**]{.underline}.
Trump's [tariff **ultimatum** forces exactly this kind of impossible
choice]{.underline}.

The next six months will reveal whether Lee's pragmatic gamble can
deliver both market performance and structural progress when success
depends largely on forces beyond his control. The stakes extend beyond
South Korean politics to questions about democratic capitalism's ability
to address [long-term challenges]{.underline} while satisfying
short-term political demands in an [increasingly **constrained** global
environment]{.underline}. Lee's presidency may ultimately prove that in
a globalized economy, domestic political leadership matters less than
the external forces that determine national economic fate.

[of]{.underline} modern [Russian air defense systems. Nor was it a

#### No East Asian prolif\-\--U.S. can reassure.

Nicholas **Miller 24**, Associate Professor of Government at Dartmouth

As discussed above, [the US has a **successful track record** of

Declaration" provides for enhanced consultation on matters of nuclear
deterrence, closer operational planning at the conventional level, and
visits to South Korea by nuclear-armed US submarines. As part of the

commitment to remain non-nuclear.108 The Biden administration likewise
strengthened its commitment to Japan, endorsing Tokyo's decision to
substantially expand its military capabilities.109 The US, South Korea,

[Even if Japanese or South Korean leaders are not fully

alternative]{.underline}---[an independent nuclear weapons
program]{.underline}---[is]{.underline} likely to be perceived as [**too

substantial [nuclear energy]{.underline} programs, [could **cause the US

[While some have argued the US would]{.underline} (or should) [look the

with the argument above that intense geopolitical competition outweighs
nonproliferation concerns, [this is **unlikely**]{.underline} for

### No Prolif---Framing---SoKo

#### Reject [naïve optimism]{.underline}. There are [overwhelming]{.underline} barriers to prolif.

Dr. Chung‑in **Moon &** Young‑Deok **Shin 23**, PhD, Distinguished
University Professor, Political Science & International Relations,
Yonsei University. Krause Distinguished Fellow, School of Policy &
Global Strategy, University of California, San Diego. Co-Convener,
Asia-Pacific Leadership Network for Nuclear Non-Proliferation &
Disarmament. Editor-In-Chief, Global Asia. Former Special Advisor,
Foreign Affairs & National Security, President Moon Jae-in; Fellow,
Global Asia, East Asia Foundation, \"'South Korea Going Nuclear?':
Debates, Driving Forces, and Prospects,\" China International Strategy
Review, Vol. 5, pg. 11-12, 11/28/2023, Springer.

The nuclear taboo worked in the past, thanks to international safeguard
regulations that strictly prohibit reprocessing of spent fuels and
enrichment of weapons-grade uranium. Seoul has faithfully complied with
them. But on April 28, 2023, President **[Yoon]{.underline}** [made
another statement]{.underline} at the Harvard Kennedy School [that "If
committed]{.underline}, **[So]{.underline}**uth **[Ko]{.underline}**rea
[has technological infrastructure for nuclear armament in]{.underline}
**[less than one year]{.underline}**." His remarks sparked domestic
debates on South Korea's technological capabilities to produce weapons
(Lim 2023). Yoon's [position reflected the view of]{.underline} Seo
**[Kyun-ryul]{.underline}**, a professor emeritus of nuclear engineering
at the Seoul National University [who has been arguing]{.underline} that
[South Korea has]{.underline} **[technical]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} **[financial]{.underline}** [capabilities to produce
**nuc**lear weapon**s** in 1 year]{.underline}. He asserted that South
Korea has already acquired technology for production of fissile
materials through uranium enrichment and separation of plutonium, while
achieving cutting-edge technology for high explosives, detonation, and
guidance system. For him, "nuclear weapons development in South Korea is
not a matter of technology and economy. If the state makes the decision,
and politicians provide a political shield, mass production of nuclear
weapons will not be difficult" (SPN Editorial 2017).

Seo's projection is not groundless. In 2016, Charles Ferguson,
then-president of the Federation of American Scientists, estimated that
South Korea has up to 4,330 bombs' worth of plutonium at the Wolsong
nuclear reactor site, assuming a conservative estimate of about 6 kg of
plutonium for a first-generation fission device (Ferguson 2016, 70).2
Hecker, a renowned nuclear scientist and former head of the Los Alamos
Laboratory, also concurs that South Korea can produce nuclear bombs in a
relatively short period. [But]{.underline} nuclear [weaponization
requires a full set of]{.underline} **[nuclear
facilities]{.underline}**, **[weapons-grade fissile
materials]{.underline}**, verification through **[nuclear
testing]{.underline}**, [and a full range of]{.underline} **[delivery
vehicles]{.underline}**. [The process to]{.underline}
**[develop]{.underline}** [these is]{.underline}
**[costly]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[time-consuming]{.underline}**. He also [questions which]{.underline}
**[provinces]{.underline}** [would allow]{.underline}
**[underground]{.underline}** [nuclear testing sites]{.underline}
(Hecker 2021). Moreover, [there are]{.underline} **[several
barriers]{.underline}**. [South Korea has]{.underline}
**[not]{.underline}** [fully acquired technology for]{.underline}
**[reprocessing]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} **[uranium
enrichment]{.underline}**. [Research on nuclear]{.underline}
**[detonation]{.underline}** [devices and the production of]{.underline}
**[nuclear warheads]{.underline}** [has been suspended for]{.underline}
**[more than 40 years]{.underline}**. [Despite]{.underline} having [a
rich human resource pool related to the]{.underline}
**[peaceful]{.underline}** [use of nuclear energy, South Korea
lacks]{.underline} **[qualified researchers]{.underline}** [in the field
of nuclear]{.underline} **[weapons]{.underline}** [development after
the]{.underline} Agency for Defense Development (**[ADD]{.underline}**)
[disbanded]{.underline} its nuclear weapons research unit [in the late
1970s]{.underline}. Thus, [developing nuclear weapons will
be]{.underline} quite [a]{.underline} **[daunting]{.underline}**
[challenge]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} **[prudent]{.underline}**
[assessment notwithstanding, conservative politicians and pundits
are]{.underline} **[jumping]{.underline}** [on]{.underline} this
**[nuclear optimism]{.underline}** and pushing for the independent
nuclear path. Some pundits have even urged the South Korean government
to embark on the second Manhattan project for nuclear weapons
development (Kim 2023a, b, c, d).

#### They need to win South Korea gets a [whole arsenal]{.underline}. 

Eric **Brewer et al. 23**, Deputy Vice President, Nuclear Threat
Initiative. Former Director, Counterproliferation, National Security
Council. Former Deputy, National Intelligence Office for WMD &
Proliferation, National Intelligence Council; Toby Dalton, PhD,
Co-Director & Senior Fellow, Nuclear Policy Program, Carnegie Endowment
for International Peace; Kylie Jones, James C. Gaither Junior Fellow,
Nuclear Policy Program, Carnegie Endowment for International Peace,
\"Mind the Gaps: Reading South Korea's Emergent Proliferation
Strategy,\" The Washington Quarterly, Vol. 46, Issue 2, 149-150,
07/13/2023, T&F.

The fourth thread relevant to [proliferation strategy in]{.underline}
**[So]{.underline}**uth **[Ko]{.underline}**rean nuclear weapons
**[discourse]{.underline}** [centers on achieving an]{.underline}
**[arsenal]{.underline}** [that would yield the]{.underline} **[desired
strategic objectives]{.underline}** [vis-à-vis]{.underline}
**[No]{.underline}**rth **[Ko]{.underline}**rea. Nuclear weapons
[advocates recognize that]{.underline} **[So]{.underline}**uth
**[Ko]{.underline}**rea [would]{.underline} **[need more than a single
weapon]{.underline}**, which according to Korea Advanced Institute of
Science & Technology (KAIST) professor Yim Man-sung could take somewhere
between six months to two years to develop.31 [Rather, Seoul would need
to establish the infrastructure to create an]{.underline}
**[arsenal]{.underline}** [of sufficient size and capability to
meet]{.underline} **[posited]{.underline}** [deterrence
objectives]{.underline}. This issue is not usually stated clearly, but
[it is implied in some ideas about needing to]{.underline} match or
**[exceed No]{.underline}**rth
**[Ko]{.underline}**rea**['s]{.underline}** nuclear
[arsenal]{.underline}. Estimates vary, but North Korea is generally
believed to possess enough nuclear material for 40 to 60 nuclear
weapons.32

Considerations on building an arsenal are driven by an emerging belief
that South Korea can easily catch up to and surpass North Korean nuclear
capabilities. That is, Seoul is better postured---due to economic
resources, its advanced technological industries, and its partnerships
with the United States and other international powers---to "win" an
ensuing arms race with North Korea.33 Although it is unclear whether he
was thinking in terms of a single weapon or a nuclear arsenal, this
confidence is reflected in President Yoon's comments in January 2023,
when he stated that the country's "advanced science and technology"
would allow it to build nuclear weapons"sooner rather than later,"if
needed. There even appears to be some appetite for arms racing as a way
to force Pyongyang to capitulate and agree to arms reductions. The
Sejong Institute's Cheong Seong-Chang, for example, argues that
"Pyongyang cannot unreasonably \[sic\] continue its nuclear arms race
with Seoul, which possesses more than 4,000 nuclear bombs' worth of
fissile material. In the end, when the DPRK realizes its limitations,
interKorean military dialogue can begin, aiming at the suspension of the
nuclear arms race and reduction of nuclear weapons." 34 Other experts
have gone even higher in their size estimates of a potential arsenal.35
It seems doubtful that South Korean officials would decide to produce
thousands of nuclear weapons, but the idea of a sufficient capacity for
a large arsenal is clearly important.

Interestingly, ROK nuclear advocates focus on the country's substantial
stockpile of plutonium-bearing spent nuclear power reactor fuel, which
if reprocessed could serve as the basis for this arsenal capacity. All
of this plutonium is reactor grade, with the spent fuel having been used
in nuclear power reactors optimized for electricity generation.
Technically, it is possible to manufacture nuclear weapons from
reactor-grade plutonium. However, [as]{.underline} plutonium metallurgy
expert and former director of Los Alamos National Laboratory Sigfried
**[Hecker]{.underline}** [points out, there is an]{.underline}
**[important difference]{.underline}** [between demonstrating
the]{.underline} **[technical feasibility of a single
weapon]{.underline}** [and building an]{.underline} **[entire
arsenal]{.underline}** [with reactor-grade plutonium]{.underline}.36
[Every other state with nuclear weapons has built
facilities]{.underline} **[specifically]{.underline}** [for the purpose
of churning out]{.underline} **[weapons-grade plutonium]{.underline}**,
which is [irradiated for]{.underline} much **[shorter]{.underline}**
[periods to minimize the]{.underline} **[concentration]{.underline}**
[of isotopes that]{.underline} **[complicate
weaponization]{.underline}**. In other words, even [though arsenal
capacity seems to be an]{.underline} **[important consideration in
So]{.underline}**uth **[Ko]{.underline}**rean **[nuclear
discourse]{.underline}**, [it is not yet backed by a]{.underline}
**[clear]{.underline}**ly articulated [technical strategy]{.underline},
at least publicly.

### No Prolif---Impossible---SoKo 

#### Prolif impossible.

**Atkinson '25** \[Joel; June 25; PhD, is a Lecturer in Taiwan Studies
at Monash University in Melbourne, Australia; The Strategist, "Why South
Korea won't build the bomb,"
https://www.aspistrategist.org.au/why-south-korea-wont-build-the-bomb/;
accessed: 7.16.25\]

The [idea of **South Korea**]{.underline} developing [**nuc**lear
weapon**s** is reaching critical mass]{.underline}, if you'll excuse the
pun. Surveys show as much as 70 percent of the public now in favor.
There has also been plenty of commentary on why it's necessary: North
Korea's growing arsenal, China's rise, and the United States' perceived
increasing unreliability. The feedback buzz between public opinion and
commentary has grown so much that the issue became a presidential
campaign topic.

However, [just because something is popular and there are good reasons
for it doesn't mean politicians will **deliver** it]{.underline}. [Both
sides of politics have their **reservations**]{.underline}. The left
sees it as [harmful to relations with Pyongyang]{.underline} and [at
odds with the goal of **denuclearising** the north]{.underline}, while
those on the right [fear alliance **pushback**]{.underline}, [damage to
**exports**]{.underline}, and [weakening of **relations** with
China]{.underline}.

But the more [**fundamental obstacle** is that]{.underline} going
nuclear would require consistent unity of national purpose: [an
executive must **decide** to do it]{.underline}, a [legislature must
**pay** for it]{.underline}, [and the apparatus of government must
**facilitate** it]{.underline}. These efforts [must then be
**sustained** long enough to make it happen]{.underline}. But [**unity**
is a typically **scarce commodity** in South Korean politics, and is
certainly nowhere to be seen now]{.underline}. Politics has been nothing
if not [turbulent over the past 10 years]{.underline}.

The country remains relatively functional and maintains bipartisan focus
on certain policy approaches. Still, embarking on something new and
ambitious, such as a nuclear weapons program, is different to merely
keeping on. It requires a novel and sustained confluence of ideological
currents.

In South Korea, North Korea is an ideological fulcrum. The extreme right
wants to liberate the north at the risk of war, while the furthest left
wants the north's regime to reign over the entire peninsula. Politics
happens in the middle. The left wants to accommodate the north's
preferences on the way to cooperative unification, while the right
insists that the north must accommodate the preferences of the
south---including denuclearisation---if cooperation is to proceed.

The [left now holds both the **legislature** and the
**presidency**]{.underline}. Security plays a crucial role in outreach
to the north, as it provides the South Korean public with the confidence
needed to pursue cooperative peace, or even unification. But those on
the left [believe that security can come from the US **alliance** and
from South Korea's considerable **conventional
capabilities**]{.underline}. In their view, [nuclear weapons are not
only unnecessary; they would disrupt **engagement** with Pyongyang and
derail **the agenda internationally**]{.underline}.

The [right]{.underline} doesn't get to decide matters of state anymore.
But even if missteps by the left gave it that power in the future, it
would [balk at nuclearisation]{.underline}. For the right, the [US

#### It wouldn't happen---sanctions 

**Reyero '23** \[Maria; October 4; Litigation Intern; Global Affairs,
"The nuclear proliferation threat in the Korean Peninsula: Moving
towards a South Korean nuclearization?",
https://www.unav.edu/web/global-affairs/the-nuclear-proliferation-threat-in-the-korean-peninsula-moving-towards-a-south-korean-nuclearization-;
accessed: 7.16.25\]

Is it really feasible for South Korea on its own?

In addition to the [lack of willingness of **Washington** to **assist**
South Korea in a possible nuclear **program**]{.underline}, [67% of
South Koreans]{.underline}, when asked in a recent poll, confessed to
[prefer]{.underline} an independent capability and [not so much to
**rely** on the United States if the time comes to develop their own
**nukes**]{.underline}.

In technological terms, it is important to state that South Korea has
what is known as 'nuclear latency' meaning it has the possession of
"some or all of the technologies, facilities, materials, expertise
(including tacit knowledge), resources, and other capabilities needed to
develop nuclear weapons"; a clear indicator of how it could develop
nuclear weapons if desired, or at least, a good first step towards that
objective. In fact, its own president announced that "we can have our
own nuclear weapons pretty quickly, given our scientific and
technological capabilities". Moreover, the country is one of the top
global producers of nuclear energy and has 25 nuclear power reactors.
[There are, however, some **flaws** in South Korea's **nuclear
infrastructure**, added to the fact that the country lacks **uranium**
and some of the requiring capacities to work with it]{.underline}.

Another important facet to consider is that [South Korea is a state
party to the **N**uclear Non-**p**roliferation **T**reaty]{.underline}
(1968) since 1975. Due to this fact, [if the government decided to
acquire nuclear weapons, the country would have to withdraw from the
treaty]{.underline}, thus [losing **international
prestige**]{.underline} and reputation, since [South Korea has always
been a **strong voice** advocating for nuclear
**disarmament**]{.underline}. The Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty bans
all countries except for the five recognized nuclear weapon states from
owning nuclear arms. In exchange, the countries possessing nuclear
weapons have to fulfill the obligation to aid non-nuclear states, such
as South Korea, in developing peaceful nuclear energy, as well as to
promote global nuclear disarmament.

This [treaty is refraining many other actors from becoming nuclear,
being of **utmost importance** when it comes to trust-building between
states and global security]{.underline}. If South Korea breaks or leaves
the treaty, it [may even be subject to harsh **sanctions** and
**repercussions**]{.underline}, despite it being a right to leave
(article 10) for "extraordinary events, related to the subject matter of
this treaty, have jeopardized the supreme interests of its country". A
three months\' notice must then be given to the UN General Assembly, for
it to decide whether the claim is legitimate and in accordance with the
right of withdrawal or not, and it is not very clear whether a possible
South Korean nuclear acquisition could fit in. As a matter of fact,
North Korea is the only country that has ever left said treaty, back in
2003.

#### It [explicitly disproves]{.underline} causality.

Azusa **Katagiri 23**, Associate Professor, Osaka School of

the cases of Germany and Japan in the postwar period. However, as seen
with Taiwan (1967, 1987) and South Korea (1959, 1970), the security
guarantor's dilemma always exists between a nuclear defender and its

This section proceeds to present results from the supply-side equation.

development tends to pay off despite inherent technological
difficulties. The results also imply that the more seriously a state
develops nuclear weapons (pursuit rather than exploration), the more
likely it is to achieve technological milestones.

#### AND [inverts]{.underline} public support warrants.

Dr. Stephen **Herzog &** Dr. Lauren **Sukin 23**, PhD, Senior
Researcher, Nuclear Arms Control, Center for Security Studies, ETH
Zurich; PhD, Assistant Professor, International Relations, Department of
International Relations, London School of Economics and Political
Science, \"The Dueling Nuclear Nightmares Behind the South Korean
President's Alarming Comments,\" Carnegie Endowment for International
Peace, 01/25/2023,
https://carnegieendowment.org/2023/01/25/dueling-nuclear-nightmares-behind-south-korean-president-s-alarming-comments-pub-88879.

To acquire its own nuclear weapons, **[So]{.underline}**uth
**[Ko]{.underline}**rea [would have to]{.underline}
**[withdraw]{.underline}** [from the NPT]{.underline}. The 1968 treaty
bans members other than Britain, China, France, Russia, and the United
States from possessing nuclear arms. In exchange, the nuclear-haves
promise to assist the have-nots with nuclear energy capabilities and to
work toward disarmament. The treaty has played a major role preventing
proliferation for more than five decades.

What had been fringe discussions over whether South Korea should leave
the NPT or prioritize conventional contingency planning with the United
States have become mainstream discourse as the security environment
deteriorates. To date, North Korea is the only state to have withdrawn
from the NPT, although nuclear-armed India, Israel, and Pakistan never
joined. The treaty permits withdrawal in "extraordinary circumstances"
if states provide a three-month notice, giving South Korea a theoretical
exit strategy. But if every state with significant security concerns
withdrew, preventing additional proliferation would prove impossible.
Washington must emphasize that a precedent-setting NPT exit by Seoul is
unacceptable.

After all, Seoul and Washington have recommended punishing states that
withdraw by terminating all civilian nuclear cooperation. [NPT
withdrawers]{.underline} would then [have to]{.underline} **[return
materials and technology]{.underline}** [to their foreign
suppliers]{.underline}. If South Korea were held to its own standard[,

### No Prolif---Demographics---SoKo 

#### Demographic shift.

**Kim '24** \[James; April 16; Assistant Professor in the Department of
Political Science at the University of Washington; MIT Center for
International Studies, "Dousing South Korea's nuclear desires,"
https://cis.mit.edu/news/dousing-south-koreas-nuclear-desires; accessed:
7.16.25\]

[South Korea elected its first **post-war generation** president in
2022]{.underline} and this [trend is expected to
**continue**]{.underline}. [As the war generation's **influence**
declines, South Korea will be **less determined** to develop its own
nuclear weapons]{.underline}.

Undoubtedly, South Koreans' current support for nuclear armament goes
beyond just the war generation. But the [war generation are the ones who
provide **unwavering support** and their position on nuclear armament is
less affected by **external factors**.]{.underline} [Younger cohorts are
more likely to **strategically alter** their views]{.underline} about
nuclear weapons in response to the evolving security environment.
According to a recent Korea Institute for National Unification report,
[more than **half** of respondents **withdrew** their **support** for
nuclear development after learning about its **costs**]{.underline},
such as the possibility of economic sanctions on South Korea.

As the war generation declines, South Koreans' views on nuclear armament
will become increasingly contingent on the security environment. This
dependence will open up the possibility that [US and South Korean
**policy decisions** may be able to somewhat control the South Korean
public's desire for nuclear weapons]{.underline}.

### No Prolif---Public Support---SoKo

#### Public support warrant is [wrong]{.underline}.

Dr. Hwee-Rhak **Park 24**, PhD, Professor, Special Affairs, Kookmin
University. Visiting Professor, University of the Philippines. Visiting
Scholar, Center for Strategic Studies, Fletcher School of Law &
Diplomacy, Tufts University, \"A Non-Nuclear US Ally's Nuclear
Preparedness Dilemma: South Korea's 'Three-Axis System',\" Defense
Studies, pg. 6-7, 01/11/2024, T&F.

South [Korea]{.underline} **[once]{.underline}** [pursued its own
nuclear armament]{.underline} in the 1970s in response to the US's
unilateral decision to withdraw its military forces from its territory,
but immediately stopped it as the US cancelled the decision (Lim 2019,
307). Spurred by this experience, 50--70% of South Koreans consistently
supported this option in the surveys from 2010 (Dalton, Friedhoff, and
Kim 2022, 5--6). When North Korea developed its first nuclear weapon in
2013, a few South Korean conservative party leaders publicly and
strongly demanded to pursue this option (Bandow 2016). This demand was
repeated whenever the North Korean nuclear threat exacerbated. Even the
current South Korean President Yoon Suk-yeol himself mentioned the
necessity of reviewing this option in early 2023 (Choe 2023), when 76.6%
of South Koreans turned out to support this option (Jung 2023). A few US
scholars recommended this option, too (Bandow 2016, 2022; Lind and Press
2021).

**[So]{.underline}**uth **[Ko]{.underline}**rea, however, [does not seem
to have the]{.underline} **[opportunity or willingness]{.underline}**
[to pursue its nuclear armament option]{.underline}. [It is]{.underline}
**[very probable]{.underline}** [for]{.underline}
**[No]{.underline}**rth **[Ko]{.underline}**rea [to conduct
a]{.underline} **[preventive]{.underline}** [nuclear attack]{.underline}
if South Korea is to develop nuclear weapons. [No South Koreans can
be]{.underline} **[sure]{.underline}** that [their cost, including
the]{.underline} **[withdrawal]{.underline}** [of the US NED, will be
worth risking]{.underline}. Most of all, South Korea's [potential for
nuclear armament is]{.underline} **[very low]{.underline}**, as Stephan
Herzog (2020, 76) evaluated South Korea at 5.2 on a ten-point scale in
2019, Japan at 7.8, and India and Pakistan at 9.4 and 7.8 in 1998 (the
year when the two countries conducted successful nuclear explosion
tests). Actually, [the]{.underline} South Korean [government
has]{.underline} **[not pursued]{.underline}** [this option]{.underline}
**[despite]{.underline}** [continuous demands by its people
and]{.underline} a few [politicians]{.underline}.

In theory, nuclear civil defence is indispensable. In reality, however,
[it became]{.underline} **[difficult for democratic
countries]{.underline}** [of the post-Cold War era to]{.underline}
**[execute]{.underline}** [civil defence]{.underline}. The [**So**uth
**Ko**rean government even]{.underline} **[did not]{.underline}** [join
the]{.underline} **[nuclear]{.underline}** [civil defence drills
of]{.underline} Hawaii, [the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates, [and]{.underline} Tokyo,
**[Japan]{.underline}**, in 2018 [when]{.underline}
**[No]{.underline}**rth **[Ko]{.underline}**rea [test-fired long-range
missiles]{.underline}. Although [the]{.underline} current
**[Yoon]{.underline}** [administration]{.underline} conducted the drill,
it [had to]{.underline} **[reduce the scope]{.underline}** [only to
government officials]{.underline} on 16 May 2023, [due to negative
public response, and its second]{.underline} drill for the general
public on 23 August [was]{.underline} almost **[neglected]{.underline}**
[by the people]{.underline} (Shin and Park 2023). The South Korean
government and military have to prepare for the worst-case scenarios
without active participation from the public.

#### Elites [in control]{.underline} reject prolif.

Sangyong **Son &** Jong H. **Park 23**, PhD, Associate Professor, School
of Civil, Architecture & Environmental Engineering, Korea University;
JD, MA, Attorney, PL Law Group LLC, \"Nonproliferation Information and
Attitude Change: Evidence from South Korea,\" Journal of Conflict
Resolution, Vol. 67, Issue 6, pg. 1096-1097, 2023, SAGE. \[italics in
original\]

The [majority of]{.underline} previous [research on nuclear
proliferation]{.underline} has [focused on]{.underline} **[elite
motivations]{.underline}** [for acquiring or abstaining from nuclear
weapons, with]{.underline} **[little attention]{.underline}** [to
the]{.underline} **[importance]{.underline}** [of public
opinion]{.underline}.1 Recent studies, however, have increasingly
focused on the role of public opinion in shaping elite decisions on
nuclear security policy (Berger 2014; Cole 1997; Flynn and Rattinger
1985). In this shift to public opinion in nuclear proliferation
research, an increasing number of scholars are focusing on South Korea
for its potential contributions to both studying and deriving policy
implications for nuclear proliferation and nonproliferation (Ko 2019;
Sukin 2019; Son and Yim 2021).

The [foreign policy gap between]{.underline} **[elites]{.underline}**
[and the]{.underline} **[public]{.underline}** [over nuclear
proliferation]{.underline} **[in South Korea]{.underline}** [has been an
intriguing puzzle]{.underline} for both theorists and practitioners of
international security. [Public support]{.underline} for the acquisition
of nuclear weapons **[consistently]{.underline}** [ranged
between]{.underline} **[50 and 70%]{.underline}** in more than 20
opinion polls conducted in South Korea (Dalton and Han 2020, 5). South
Korean public support for proliferation serves as evidence of "what
people really want" to international audiences. For example, Lind and
Press (2021a, 2021b) recently argued that South Korea should be allowed
to develop its own nuclear weapons in order to protect itself from North
Korea's nuclear threat, claiming that "70% of the South Korean public
(already) supports the move" (Lind and Press, 2021b).2 By contrast, [a
survey of 50 South Korean national security experts shows]{.underline}
that **[64%]{.underline}** of them [are opposed to independent nuclear
weapons development, and only]{.underline} **[12%]{.underline}**
[supported]{.underline}.3

In this paper, we examine whether [experts']{.underline} **[credible
nonproliferation information]{.underline}** [about the]{.underline}
potential **[consequences]{.underline}** of [nuclear weapons
development]{.underline} (hereinafter referred to as "nonproliferation
information") [can]{.underline} **[change]{.underline}** pro-armament
[voters']{.underline} pre-existing [attitudes]{.underline} and reduce
the foreign policy gap in nuclear proliferation. More specifically, we
compare the relative effectiveness of different types of
nonproliferation information derived from existing nonproliferation
studies: economic sanctions, conditional military sanctions, nuclear
technology sanctions, normative sanctions, reduced security threats,
elite and public opposition to nuclear proliferation. To evaluate the
effects of these seven types of nonproliferation information, we devise
a novel experiment on attitude change, where attitude change is defined
as a change in both opinion and behavior. In the opinion change
experiment, which is fielded at the beginning of the survey,
pro-armament subjects are randomly assigned to one of seven types of
nonproliferation information that is briefed to be written by South
Korean foreign policy experts. In the behavior change experiment
conducted after the opinion change experiment, pro-armament subjects are
asked to allocate donation money to either pro- or anti-armament
organizations. The initial experiment was conducted in December 2019 and
was repeated in January 2022 to assess the sensitivity of the initial
findings to changes in the internal and external experimental
conditions.

The [findings]{.underline} of our experiments **[clearly
show]{.underline}** that [experts' nonproliferation information had
a]{.underline} **[considerable impact]{.underline}** [on pro-armament
subjects' attitude changes]{.underline}. In the 2019 and 2022
experiments, an average of 53.4% of pro-armament subjects changed their
opinions and 16.8% of them changed their behaviors after receiving
credible nonproliferation information. Economic sanctions information
has the largest treatment effect on pro-armament subjects' attitude
change in both the 2019 and 2022 experiments. When given economic
sanctions information, an average of 77% of proarmament subjects changed
their opinions and 24% of them changed their behaviors in both
experiments. This finding suggests that the pro-armament public may
change their pre-existing attitudes if they learn about the consequences
of postarmament economic sanctions, which we call the *enlightening
effect of economic sanctions information*.

#### The public is [against]{.underline} prolif.

Robert **Kelly 22**; July 15; professor of political science at Pusan

[**theft**, **loss**, **rogue scientists**, and so]{.underline} on are
legitimate fears. But they are no more resonant with South Korea or
Japan than with any other nuclear weapons state. Indeed, [as liberal
**democracies** with **robust state capacities** and preexisting,
well-managed]{.underline} [**nuclear energy** programs, they will likely
be quite **responsible**]{.underline}, as Britain and France have been.

[No one]{.underline} seriously [believes Seoul or Tokyo will launch an
**out-of-the-blue**, nuclear-first strike]{.underline} on an opponent;
[set up]{.underline} something like the A.Q. Khan [**proliferation**
network; **sell WMDs**]{.underline} to terrorists or other rogues; [put
Homer **Simpson** in charge of]{.underline} nuclear [safety; or
be]{.underline} so **[sloppy]{.underline}** as to require something like
the Nunn-Lugar program. Even Pakistan and India have been better with
their arsenals than the panic of the late 1990s suggested. Even
[dictatorships have been **cautious** about these issues]{.underline}.
And as [**democracies** with a history of **foreign-policy
restraint**]{.underline}, democratic peace theory [suggests they would
be **good stewards**]{.underline}, certainly better than East Asia's
autocratic nuclear powers.

There is [generalized anxiety about a regional **arms
race**]{.underline}, which South Korean/Japanese nuclearization might
exacerbate. Perhaps, but as noted above, [there is **no local cascade**
to be sparked because it has already occurred. **China**, **Russia**,
and **No**]{.underline}rth **[Ko]{.underline}**rea [have all moved
first]{.underline}. China and Russia have established nuclear arsenals
[and **no intention** of complying with the build-down imperative.
Russia's]{.underline} growing [rhetorical **invocation**]{.underline} of
its nuclear weapons [is a disturbing evolution. No]{.underline}rth
[Ko]{.underline}rea repeatedly agreed, non-bindingly since 1992, to
avoid nuclear weapons---only to [**exit** the **NPT** and keep
building]{.underline}. It now has intercontinental ballistic missiles
and several dozen nuclear warheads.

# Japan Disadvantage 

## Top Level 

### 1NC---Japan Disadvantage 

#### The Alliance is [strong now]{.underline} despite tariffs, but the plan would [shock]{.underline} Japan and [cause them to proliferate]{.underline}---maintaining the [stable alliance]{.underline} is key

Hal **Brands 25**, senior fellow at the American Enterprise Institute,
where he studies US foreign policy and defense strategy, the Henry A.
Kissinger Distinguished Professor of Global Affairs at the Johns Hopkins
School of Advanced International Studies (SAIS), "Japan's Biggest Fear
Isn't China or North Korea. It's the US.", *American Enterprise
Institute*,
<https://www.aei.org/op-eds/japans-biggest-fear-isnt-china-or-north-korea-its-the-us/>,
June 4, 2025

The [US-Japan alliance has never been more important or
stronger]{.underline}. And [rarely has it faced]{.underline} greater
[uncertainties]{.underline} or dangers. [That's]{.underline} the
[paradoxical]{.underline} takeaway from my trip last week to Tokyo,
where I spoke to government and private-sector audiences about the state
of the world.

[Japan faces growing threats]{.underline} in its neighborhood,
[from]{.underline} a bellicose [China and]{.underline} a potentially
provocative [North Korea.]{.underline} But [the state of America was the
foremost worry]{.underline} of nearly everyone I met.

[Washington's alliance with Japan has been an inspiring]{.underline}
reminder that friendship can follow bitter enmity. [It has anchored the
US security presence]{.underline} in the Pacific for decades and looms
ever-larger today. [The risk of conflict in that region is rising, and
America's military edge is slipping]{.underline}. That means [Washington
will need greater support from its friends]{.underline} --- and [it
makes Japan]{.underline}, [America's most powerful ally]{.underline} in
the world's most crucial region, utterly [indispensable]{.underline}.

On many dimensions, **[the relationship is thriving]{.underline}**.
[Japan is muscling up militarily, by planning to nearly double defense
spending between 2022 and 2027]{.underline}. [Tokyo and
Washington]{.underline} are gradually turning what was once a deeply
asymmetrical alliance --- [a strategic protectorate]{.underline}, really
--- into a genuine warfighting partnership.

[Tokyo has also made itself a vital regional asset]{.underline} by
[engaging countries in Southeast Asia,]{.underline} where US ties are
sometimes strained. In [strengthening security partnerships]{.underline}
with India, Australia and the Philippines, and joining a host of
trilateral and quadrilateral arrangements, [Japan has become a crucial
node in an emerging regional security network.]{.underline}

There are [challenges on the horizon]{.underline}. Japan may struggle to
push defense spending past 2% of gross domestic product after 2027, let
alone reach the 5% standard that Secretary of Defense Pete Hegseth
endorsed in his recent speech to the Shangi-La Dialogue in Singapore.
[Japanese officials welcome affirmations]{.underline} that the Pacific
is America's priority theater, [but they wonder,]{.underline} following
yet another US military intervention in the Middle East, [whether the
elusive pivot will ever]{.underline} really [happen]{.underline}.
Meanwhile, [intensive security cooperation coexists with profound
**anxiety**]{.underline} [about where the larger relationship might
go.]{.underline}

The [biggest headaches are economic.]{.underline} President Donald Trump
rightly won plaudits for OKing Nippon Steel's acquisition of US Steel,
after his predecessor, Joe Biden, had stymied the deal on made-up
national security grounds. [But Trump's tariff proposals have been
particularly galling to the Japanese]{.underline}, who had cut a new
trade pact with the president in his first term and were hoping that as
America's most important ally, [they wouldn't be treated as a commercial
enemy.]{.underline}

A deal to prevent Trump from reimposing the worst levies seems likely:
Japan can't afford a trade war with the US amid the threat of a real war
with China. [But there is a strong sense that the US is abandoning the
open global economy it once anchored]{.underline}.

In remarks at the Nikkei Future of Asia Forum last week, both Prime
Minister Shigeru Ishiba and Foreign Minister Takeshi Iwaya argued that
Japan and its neighbors must cooperate to defend free trade. The
unspoken implication was that the country attacking it is the US.

Then there are the political perplexities, on both sides. Ishiba's
minority government seems likely to lose seats in upcoming parliamentary
elections, which could bring out the knives for an unpopular prime
minister. But regardless of who holds that job, Japan will probably have
weak leaders for the foreseeable future. That will hinder efforts to
push through ambitious economic or security reforms, let alone grapple
with a potentially disastrous demographic decline.

[If Japan's politics are uninspiring, America's seem downright
befuddlin]{.underline}g. A superpower that has long provided global
peace and prosperity is now threatening to grab land from its allies and
is whipsawing the world economy with capricious, ill-considered policy
decisions.

[Trump's administration is attacking the innovation
ecosystem]{.underline} and inflows of global talent that underpin US
economic primacy. It is slashing the national security infrastructure
that helps a president handle dangerous crises. Most fundamentally, [the
two-time triumph of a man so ambivalent toward US allies and global
engagement unavoidably raises questions about whether Trump's presidency
marks the start of a longer America First era, in which Washington
rejects the international order it once led.]{.underline}

[It's hard to overstate how jarring that would be for
Japan.]{.underline} It recovered from World War II under American
patronage and protection. [Japanese officials know that the **regional
balance will buckle** if Washington **pulls back** as Beijing surges
forward.]{.underline} For decades, t[he alliance with America has been
the bedrock of Japanese foreign policy. If that alliance ever falters,
drastic measures --- like **building nuclear weapons** --- might be the
requirement of Japan's survival.]{.underline}

No one I spoke to thinks such a rupture is imminent. In the near term,
few countries are working with Trump's America more closely. But in the
long term, few countries are more challenged by what his presidency
might augur for America's approach to global affairs. So for now, [the
alliance is thriving --- even as Japanese strategists contemplate the
possibility of a post-American world]{.underline}.

#### Alliance high.

**McCartney '25** \[Micah; July 17; China News Reporter; Newsweek, "US
Ally Calls China Greatest Threat,"
https://www.newsweek.com/japan-calls-china-greatest-security-threat-2100266;
accessed: 7.18.25\]

**[Japan]{.underline}** has [raised concerns over China\'s expanding
**military presence** and the shifting balance of power]{.underline} in
the Asia-Pacific.

[China\'s actions]{.underline} \"[present an **unprecedented** and the
greatest strategic **challenge**]{.underline},\" the [Japanese Defense
Ministry wrote in its annual white paper]{.underline}.

Why It Matters

The report comes as Chinese President Xi Jinping pushes to complete the
People\'s Liberation Army\'s modernization by 2035 and seeks to replace
the United States as the region\'s dominant military power. China
possesses the world\'s largest navy, a vast missile arsenal, and is
rapidly building up its nuclear capabilities.

China\'s increasing coast guard patrols near the disputed,
[Tokyo-administered Senkaku Islands]{.underline}, [and continued threats
toward neighboring]{.underline} [Taiwan]{.underline} have [prompted
Japan to reinterpret its postwar constitution to allow for the
collective **self-defense** of **allies**, boost defense spending, and
pursue **closer cooperation** with its **defense treaty** partner, the
**U.S**]{.underline}.

Newsweek reached out to the Pentagon via email for comment.

What To Know

\"The international community is facing its greatest trial since World
War II and entering a new era of crisis,\" Japanese Minister of Defense
Gen Nakatani said in the white paper presented to the Cabinet on
Tuesday.

While the 534-page [report also highlighted]{.underline} Russia\'s
military activities and North Korea\'s frequent missile launches and
advancing nuclear weapons program, it was China that was described as
the preeminent threat.

\"[Japan should respond with its comprehensive national power and in
cooperation and **collaboration** with its **ally**, **like-minded
countries**, and others]{.underline},\" the authors stated.

China\'s navy has been operating farther afield and near Japanese
territory with increasing frequency, the report said, noting that
Chinese navy warships passing between islands in Japan\'s southwestern
prefecture of Okinawa have tripled in number between 2021 and 2024.

The defense ministry expressed \"grave concern\" over recent
encroachments by China, citing the Chinese spy plane that entered
Japanese airspace, as well as the aircraft carrier Liaoning\'s passage
through the narrow gap separating Okinawa\'s Yonaguni island and Taiwan
in October.

Also featured in the report is the near-daily presence of Chinese coast
guard ships near the disputed Senkaku Islands---known as the Diaoyu
Islands in China---which the report characterized as a unilateral
attempt to change the status quo.

The report drew parallels with China\'s expansive activities in the
South China Sea, where Chinese maritime forces have drawn fire for their
increasing activities within the exclusive economic zone of the
Philippines.

China\'s strengthening security ties with Russia, including joint bomber
flights and warship patrols near Japan, described as shows of force,
continue to be a source of concern.

As for Taiwan, the self-ruled democracy China claims as its territory
and has threatened to unify by force if necessary, the defense ministry
warned that the military balance between the two sides is shifting
rapidly in Beijing\'s favor.

The paper also expressed growing concern over Beijing\'s increasing use
of gray-zone activities---coercive actions that stop short of war---and
warned that military pressure, including potential blockades, could be
used to threaten Taipei.

Although the U.S. remains Taiwan\'s primary arms supplier under the 1979
Taiwan Relations Act, Washington has maintained a policy of \"strategic
ambiguity\" regarding whether it would intervene militarily.

Many analysts believe that Japan, which views a Chinese takeover of
Taiwan as an existential threat to its national security, would likely
participate in a U.S.-led counteroffensive if conflict erupted.

What People Are Saying

Japanese Defense Minister Gen Nakatani, in the white paper: \"Under
these circumstances, Japan\...is fundamentally strengthening its defense
capabilities, including the ability to carry out counterstrike
operations, and is making steady progress on various measures, including
securing and strengthening the necessary defense budget.

\"At the same time, [Japan is working to further deepen its **alliance**
with the **U**nited **S**tates, which serves as the **cornerstone** of
its **security**, and to expand **security cooperation** with
like-minded countries and others]{.underline}.\"
