March 11 marks the 10-year anniversary of the Fukushima Daiichi nuclear
disaster, the most serious nuclear incident since the 1986 Chernobyl
catastrophe. The accident at [Fukushima debunked the myth of **nuc**lear
safety that had prevailed in Japan]{.underline} until that point. It
also threw Japan's nuclear infrastructure into a mess of uncertainty,
forcing political elites to rethink the future of energy policy.

However, **[it is doubtful that Japan will]{.underline}** fully
[**abandon nuc**lear **power.**]{.underline} This is not the result of

**nuc**lear power affects energy policies]{.underline} in ways that go
[beyond]{.underline} matters of [electricity]{.underline}.

#### Nuclear power is here to stay.

Maxime **Polleri 21**, Postdoctoral Researcher at McGill University in
the Biomedical Ethics Unit, Network Affiliate at the Center for
International Security and Cooperation at Stanford University, "Why
Japan Might Not Abandon Nuclear Power," The Diplomat, 03-11-2021,
https://thediplomat.com/2021/03/why-japan-might-not-abandon-nuclear-power/

[Fukushima led to surface level political posturing and
indecision]{.underline} around energy matters, but [**the obdurate,
enduring, and historically entrenched tropes that surround nuc**lear
**power** have **barely budged**]{.underline}. [Paying too much
attention to]{.underline} the words of [politicians can be a distraction
from this path dependency.]{.underline}

It is important to state that these tropes are not completely widespread
across Japanese society. Indeed, there was always an important
anti-nuclear movement within a part of the population. Nonetheless,
these tropes are particularly present within economic bureaucracies like
METI that have control over major policy areas. [Ten years after
Fukushima, **nuc**lear power is still considered]{.underline} a
[key]{.underline} factor [in]{.underline} issues surrounding [energy
security, global warming, **tech**nological expertise, and international
security.]{.underline}

These [issues]{.underline} did not emerge out of the blue; they [were
shaped by]{.underline} certain [historical, cultural, and political
contexts]{.underline}, which still influence the prevailing Japanese
vision and future of nuclear power. This **[legacy
represents]{.underline}** deeper **[structural factors]{.underline}**
[that supersede hectic changes in political parties]{.underline},
matters of [economic solvability, and]{.underline} the potential adverse
[health effects]{.underline} linked with radioactive contamination. It
also explains why Japan might not quit nuclear power.

Understanding the future direction of energy politics, whether in Japan
or elsewhere, means looking at how past elements remain in dialogue with
current events, often creating long-lasting influences on the governance
of nuclear power. Creating novel and more sustainable energy policies
might consequently require the toppling of enduring tropes that continue
dominate our worldviews.

###  AT//Pacifism

#### This is [factually incorrect]{.underline}\-\--Japan is [antimilitarist]{.underline} because of the [U.S. alliance]{.underline}, and a [security shock]{.underline} will have the [largest impact]{.underline} on the move [away]{.underline} from pacifism. 

Jennifer **Bradley 22**, Senior Deterrence Analyst in the Plans and
Policy Directorate at United States Strategic Command, Adjunct Professor
at the University of Nebraska at Omaha, Ph.D. candidate in Defense and
Strategic Studies at Missouri State University, M.S. in Defense and
Strategic Studies from Missouri State University, "Tailored engagement:
Assessing Japan's strategic culture and its impact on U.S. -- China
competition," Comparative Strategy, Vol. 41, No. 4, 2022,
https://doi.org/10.1080/01495933.2022.2087434

[Japan is often referred to as a pacifist country]{.underline} due to
its constitution that forbids it from waging war. However, **[a more
accurate characterization is antimilitarist]{.underline}**. Pacifist and
antimilitarist are often used interchangeably, but [pacifism suggests a
principled opposition to war while antimilitarist suggests a
**restraint** toward the use of force, limiting it to]{.underline}
situations of [self-defense]{.underline}.74 Antimilitarism has been a
norm in Japan since the United States drafted the Japanese constitution
during the occupation after World War II. Article 9 of Japan's
constitution states:

> Aspiring sincerely to an international peace based on justice and
> order, the Japanese people forever renounce war as a sovereign right
> of the nation and the threat or use of force as means of settling
> international disputes.
>
> In order to accomplish the aim of the preceding paragraph, land, sea,
> and air forces, as well as other war potential, will never be
> maintained. The right of belligerency of the state will not be
> recognized.75

Article 9 was reinterpreted almost immediately, as the United States
regretted this provision once it entered the Korean War and the Cold War
began. The pressure exerted by the United States and the realization
that with the U.S. occupied in Korea, Japan was undefended prompted the
Japanese government to reinterpret Article 9, not abandon Article 9.76
Today, [the **J**apanese **S**elf **D**efense **F**orce is a premier
military force ranking as **one of the most powerful**]{.underline}
forces [**in the world** with an annual budget around 49 billion
dollars]{.underline},77 the 9th highest in 2020.78

[The demilitarization of Japan allowed it to focus on economic
recovery]{.underline} after the devastation wrought by World War II.
However, [as Japan grew into an economic superpower a divide grew
between the government's desires to reinterpret Article 9]{.underline}
in order [to reimagine Japan's place in the international system and the
public's desire to remain bound by]{.underline} the [current
constraints]{.underline}.79 The government's argument is that Japan has
benefited greatly from the open international order and should do more
to maintain that system. Brad Glosserman describes this as a
"philosophy, which is based on the idea that Japan should contribute to
international peace, stability, and prosperity in a manner commensurate
with its economic and political standing."80

[Japan's premier military capabilities and the pride]{.underline} that
[the Japanese public takes in its Self Defense Force]{.underline}81
[**argues against the norm of pacifism** in favor of
antimilitarism]{.underline}. The debate that surrounds Japan's
antimilitarist norm is not whether or not to return to the militaristic
culture that dominated Japan prior to World War II, but how proactive
Japan should be in defending the international system that has allowed
it to develop into an economic superpower. The [changes in Japan's
security environment, or **if Japan incurs a security shock, will have
the greatest impact on the outcome of this debate**]{.underline}.82

### Impact---Japan Prolif---Regional Prolif 

#### Japan proliferation causes SoKo, Taiwan to proliferate upending regional stability

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional
Studies from the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies; Amy
Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of
Washington; Roger Clemens, B. A. in Political Economy and Global and
Regional Studies from the University of Washington; Claire Paiemont, B.
A. in International Studies and Economies from the University of
Washington; Kay Reed, B. A. in Global and Regional Studies from the
University of Washington; The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program @
the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies @ University of
Washington, \"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation\",
2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

In the [event of Japanese proliferation]{.underline}, Japan would
[**lose** any semblance of **legitimacy** as a benchmark for the
non-proliferation movement]{.underline}, and the [durable restraint of
Japan as a motivator for other countries' non-proliferation regimes
would disappear]{.underline}. When a [role model goes against the very
idea that it supported and valued, it will both **lose its adherents and
disenfranchise the ideology** itself]{.underline}. Japan is an exemplar
of advocating for and upholding non-proliferation, and if it is not
possible for Japan to remain in said position, it is unlikely that
another, less qualified country will thereafter be able to inherit it.
The [environment that this would produce is that of ideological anarchy
surrounding non-proliferation. With their benchmark defunct, countries
would **no longer feel obligated**, even on an informal basis, to
practice restraint with regard to the production of nuclear
weapons]{.underline}.8

The East Asian Disequilibrium

With the [NPT rapidly disintegrating and the ideology of
non-proliferation in a state of disrepair]{.underline}, [Japan's
immediate neighbors would be the **first to suffer from its
proliferation**]{.underline}. As it stands, Japan, South Korea (ROK),
and Taiwan (ROC) do not possess nuclear weapons despite their neighbors,
North Korea (DPRK) and China (PRC), possessing them. Rather, the nuclear
umbrella under which Japan and the ROK exist provides a counterbalance
to the nuclear capabilities of the DPRK and the PRC.9 [Japan's
acquisition of nuclear weapons would entirely **confound** this delicate
balance.]{.underline}

On the side of Japan's allies, the [ROK and ROC may **perceive** a
nuclearized Japan as a **threat** **to** their own **sovereignties**
both directly and through the threat of nuclear escalation with their
neighbors (the DPRK and PRC]{.underline}), and they may [**attempt to
match** Japan's proliferation with their own campaigns of nuclear
procurement]{.underline}. Presently, the President of the [Republic of
Korea]{.underline}, Yoon Suk-yeol has [already expressed his willingness
to begin the development of nuclear weapons]{.underline}, and --- due to
this existing willingness to proliferate --- [Japan's proliferation
would likely **serve as the catalyst** for the ROK's proprietary
development of nuclear weapons]{.underline} (Herzog and Sukin 2023).
[Regarding the ROC]{.underline}, the situation would be less dire on
account of President Tsai Ing-wen's staunchly anti-nuclear views;
nevertheless, [as non-proliferation loses its legitimacy and the ROC
gains exposure to new regional threats, this attitude would likely
change]{.underline} (AP News 2019). For Japan's nuclear neighbors, with
whom relations exist in a precarious state, its [nuclear
**proliferation** may **give** **reason** for the procurement **of**
**increasingly** **powerful** **nuclear** **arsenals** in an attempt
**to** **preempt** a potential strike from Japan and 'rebalance' the
nuclear ecosystem]{.underline}. With both effects in mind, an [**arms**
**race** among a nuclearized East Asia would occur at a
minimum]{.underline}, and --- in the worst case --- the [nuclear arms
race originating in East Asia would **spread** **sequentially** from
country to country in a centrifugal manner]{.underline}.

#### Japan proliferation greenlights revisionist powers to proliferate

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional
Studies from the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies; Amy
Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of
Washington; Roger Clemens, B. A. in Political Economy and Global and
Regional Studies from the University of Washington; Claire Paiemont, B.
A. in International Studies and Economies from the University of
Washington; Kay Reed, B. A. in Global and Regional Studies from the
University of Washington; The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program @
the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies @ University of
Washington, \"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation\",
2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

Japanese Proliferation and Revolutionary States: The Case of Iran

As previously stated, Japan's proliferation would likely lead to the
sequential centrifugal formation of nuclear states, and --- while East
Asia was explicitly mentioned previously --- [proliferation is **not
limited to Japan's immediate vicinity**; rather]{.underline}, due to the
international [scope of the]{.underline} non-proliferation regime
through the [NPT]{.underline}, the [nuclear **domino** **effect would
have an international reach**]{.underline}. While certain states may not
pose a significant threat as a result of a limited nuclear arsenal or
general nuclear abstention in the event of Japanese proliferation, other
states may pose a unique threat, and a floated set of standards for
nuclear etiquette could arise.

With the [desertion of one of the NPT's core members]{.underline}, the
legitimacy of non-proliferation would exist in a gray area, and the
enforcement of the NPT's consequences for proliferation would largely
rely on the international community's responses to acts of
proliferation10 . [If Japan were to face international sanctions, it
would suffer economically, yet it would still possess nuclear weapons.
For revolutionary states]{.underline} --- [such as Iran]{.underline} ---
upon whom sanctions have already been placed, their **[non-nuclear
status would be suboptimal]{.underline}**. As it stands, the [United
States currently has extensive sanctions placed on Iran that span over
numerous industries with merely a remote link to the Iranian nuclear
energy program]{.underline} (Nada et al. 2023). While this may serve to
reduce the scale of Iran's nuclear capabilities, it employs a great deal
of American leverage. Since the United States has levied so many
sanctions thus far, Iran's marginal loss from additional sanctions would
be greatly reduced. That being said, [Iran has already borne a **large
portion of** **international sanctions** without possessing nuclear
weapons]{.underline}; therefore, the United States and its other allies,
in an effort to materially stunt Iran's nuclear program, would not be
able to do so effectively as a result of their diminished level of
influence. [**Combined with the hypocrisy** of a supposedly pacifist
nation's decision to proliferate, Iran]{.underline} --- [and other
revolutionary states]{.underline} --- would [have both **logical and
ideological pretenses** to pursue the development of nuclear
weapons]{.underline}.

The [domino effect]{.underline}, however, is **[not]{.underline}**
entirely [**confined** **to the revolutionary states**
themselves]{.underline}. Given the [upended condition of
non-proliferation as an **ideology**]{.underline} and the presence of
the aforementioned \'gray area\', [any type of proliferation would not
have the potential to be easily contested]{.underline}. Connected with
the limited marginal loss from sanctions, revolutionary states would
have little in the way of providing nuclear weapons to ideologically
aligned terrorist states or organizations. Namely, Iran's continuous
support of designated terrorist organizations such as Hezbollah could
produce said issues in the event of Iran's reactive proliferation (Grace
and Mandelbaum 2006).11 As a major opponent of the Israeli state, a
potential nuclear power, Hezbollah's acquisition of nuclear weapons from
Iran would likely heighten regional tensions and lead to even more
clashes in the Israeli-Palestinian conflict. Further clashes with Israel
would introduce the possibility of American intervention as a result of
Israel's diplomatic ties with the United States. Additionally, as
terrorist states lack overt state affiliation, the danger of nuclear
attacks with limited retaliatory potential would have a marked increase.
As this danger increases, non-nuclear countries would have less of a
reason to retain such bounded security capabilities, thus further
augmenting the potential scale of proliferation.

Conclusion

[Japan's proliferation would]{.underline} --- as demonstrated --- [have
consequences that could **exceed initial projections**]{.underline}.
Resembling a sinking ship, the [holes in the ideology of
non-proliferation as a result of Japan's potential proliferation would
**greatly** **outnumber** the states attempting to maintain
it]{.underline}. Once this symbolic ship has sunk, there would be
[**nothing preventing the complete ideological dissolution** of an often
underappreciated security measure, and the global geopolitical
environment may **enter** an era of **nuclear disarray**]{.underline}.

### Impact---Japan Prolif---Miscalc

#### It guarantees [miscalc]{.underline}. 

Stephen **Cimbala &** Adam **Lowther 23**, Ph.D. in Political Science
from UW Madison, Distinguished Professor of Political Science at Penn
State; Ph.D. from the University of Alabama, Director of Research and
Education, Louisiana Tech Research Institute, "Nuclear Danger in Asia:
Arms Races or Stability?", Springer, June 2023, pg. 266-280

[Unstable command and control over nuclear forces could be **joined at
the hip** to **nationalist** or **religious hostility** in
Asia]{.underline}. During the Cold War, the Americans and Soviets
competed on the basis of political ideology: communism versus
capitalism. Both of these ideologies were rooted in Western philosophy
and history and neither, with the exception of lunatic fringes on both
sides, anticipated an inevitable final day of judgment between the two
systems. Despite significant differences in military strategic doctrine,
the USA and the Soviet Union established an ongoing process of nuclear
arms control that helped to stabilize their political relationship and
to avoid conflicts based on misinformation and mistaken assumptions
about one another's intentions. In a sense, more than four decades of
"nuclear learning" occurred as between the two nuclear superpowers that
lasted to the very end of the Cold War and even beyond the demise of the
USSR.7

In [Asia]{.underline}, the next decade or two [may witness the
**combination** of **absolute weapons** in the hands of leaders with
**apocalyptic motivations** or **regionally hegemonic**
objectives]{.underline}. To be clear: it is not asserted here that the
leaders of nuclear powers in Asia will be less "rational" than their
European counterparts. Rationality is a loaded, and a subjective, term.
In politics, it implies a logical or logically intended connection
between political ends and means.8 [Leaders in Asia will have political
objectives]{.underline} that differ from those of the Cold War Americans
and Soviets: not illogical in their own terms, but [**less road tested**
against accidental]{.underline}, [inadvertent]{.underline}, [or
deliberate **escalation** to nuclear war under]{.underline} the stress
of [political crisis and ambiguous intelligence]{.underline}.267

Military strategy is the realm of logical paradox and oxymoronic truths.
Strategies and policies intended as defensive can, in this paradoxical
world, appear provocative and offensive to other states. For example,
[leaders in Asia might **misconstrue** or apply mistakenly the strategy
of **preemption**]{.underline}. It is not an offensive strategy but a
defensive one. Preemption is motivated by the expectation that the
opponent has already launched an attack or is about to. It is dangerous
on account of its "defensiveness": [leaders **misperceive** that they
are already under attack based on **deficient** and **misleading
indicators** of warning]{.underline}, [or on **mistaken assumptions**
about enemy intentions and capabilities]{.underline}.9

Another possible path to war based on the twenty-first century military
realities is the deliberate use, or threatened use, of weapons of mass
destruction, including nuclear weapons. We noted already that this use
might occur as part of "anti-access" or "area denial" strategies in
Asia. [Hostile powers could employ the threat of **nuclear first use**
against American **allies**]{.underline} or forward deploying the US
forces in order [to **deter American intervention** in the
region]{.underline}, against their interests.

This is an obvious stratagem for China to use in case it decides to
forcibly disarm Taiwan or otherwise create a military fait accompli in
the Western Pacific contrary to the US interests.10 China would not need
to use nuclear or other weapons of mass destruction in order to
accomplish a number of its possible objectives in the region, absent the
US military intervention. The challenge for China would be to deter or
defeat US military intervention in favor of Taiwan or another threatened
interest. More accurate ballistic missiles of various ranges, improved
air defenses and C4ISR (command, control, communications, computers,
intelligence, surveillance and reconnaissance) and expertise in cyberwar
could augment Chinese access denial capabilities---with or without
nuclear weapons.11 In addition, [the combination of **nuclear** and
**cyber capabilities** in a **USA--China confrontation** could create a
scenario of **escalation** from conventional war into a **nuclear
crisis**]{.underline}. As Andrew Futter has noted:

[This is a particular concern given the fact that China is thought to
**share**]{.underline} some [parts of its **C2** system for **both**
nuclear and conventional forces. This risk is]{.underline}, in turn[,
likely to have implications for China's 'No First Use' nuclear
posture]{.underline}, particularly when cyber is combined with US
ballistic missile defence plans and conventional global strike
capabilities.12

### Impact---Japan Prolif---Hotspots 

#### Japan will inflame hotspots---empirics

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional

Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of

2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

The Delusion of Nuclear Security: India and Pakistan

Despite its theoretical basis, a similar scenario to that which was
previously mentioned has already materialized, and although historical
events are not necessarily indicative of the future, they can provide an
apt warning. [If Japan were to consider proliferating with the pretense
of bolstering security, a careful analysis of India and Pakistan may
serve as an appropriate deterrent]{.underline}.

Upon splitting into two separate states, [India and Pakistan have
remained in a nearly **constant** **state** **of**
**conflict**]{.underline}. Even prior to India's incipient nuclear test
in 1974, the two states had already undergone three conventional
conflicts (Hashim 2019). With the acquisition of nuclear weapons, it
could have been reasonably assumed that conflicts --- particularly
conventional conflicts --- between India and Pakistan would have ceased,
yet periodic clashes in the Kashmir region continued to occur throughout
the latter quarter of the 20th century (Hashim 2019). It is, however,
possible to argue that conflict persisted due to the absence of nuclear
weapons in Pakistan until 1998, which in theory would have eliminated
the threat of conflict due to mutually assured destruction (MAD). [From
a normative viewpoint and with **consideration** of MAD, 1998 should
have been the year in which conflict between India and Pakistan
**ceased**, yet Pakistan's proliferation proved **only to escalate**
conflict between the two states with the eruption of the Kargil conflict
in the Kashmir region during the same year]{.underline}.

[Japan]{.underline} may exist in a unique situation that currently bears
little resemblance to that between India and Pakistan; however, if
[proliferation were to occur]{.underline}, it may find itself bridging
said gap. In such an event, [Japan would occupy the position of the
**first domino in a large series**]{.underline}. By attempting to
improve its own security through nuclear sovereignty, a paradoxical
effect would transpire. [Much like Pakistan's response to India's
proliferation, Japan's proliferation would **inflame** the regional
security environment, and, as **other countries become threatened** by
Japan's proliferation, they may seek to improve their security through
the **construction of their own arsenals**, thus causing further
inflammation. As demonstrated in India and Pakistan, human conflict ---
through the age of nuclear weapons --- has persisted, and
**proliferation merely serves to augment the potential severity** of
armed conflict, not to deter it]{.underline}.

#### Especially in the Middle East 

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional
Studies from the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies; Amy
Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of
Washington; Roger Clemens, B. A. in Political Economy and Global and
Regional Studies from the University of Washington; Claire Paiemont, B.
A. in International Studies and Economies from the University of
Washington; Kay Reed, B. A. in Global and Regional Studies from the
University of Washington; The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program @
the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies @ University of
Washington, \"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation\",
2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

The [domino effect]{.underline}, however, is **[not]{.underline}**
entirely [**confined** **to the revolutionary states**
themselves]{.underline}. Given the [upended condition of
non-proliferation as an **ideology**]{.underline} and the presence of
the aforementioned \'gray area\', [any type of proliferation would not
have the potential to be easily contested]{.underline}. Connected with
the limited marginal loss from sanctions, [revolutionary states would
have **little in the way** of providing nuclear weapons to
**ideologically** **aligned** **terrorist** states or
organizations]{.underline}. Namely, Iran's continuous support of
designated terrorist organizations such as Hezbollah could produce said
issues in the event of Iran's reactive proliferation (Grace and
Mandelbaum 2006).11 [As a major opponent of the Israeli state, a
potential nuclear power, **Hezbollah's** **acquisition** of nuclear
weapons from Iran would likely **heighten** **regional** **tensions**
and lead to even more clashes in the Israeli-Palestinian
conflict]{.underline}. Further clashes with Israel would [introduce the
possibility of **American** **intervention** as a result of Israel's
diplomatic ties with the United States]{.underline}. Additionally, as
terrorist states lack overt state affiliation, the danger of nuclear
attacks with limited retaliatory potential would have a marked increase.
[As this danger increases, non-nuclear countries would have less of a
reason to retain such bounded security capabilities, thus further
augmenting the **potential** **scale** **of**
**proliferation**]{.underline}.

Conclusion

[Japan's proliferation would]{.underline} --- as demonstrated --- [have
consequences that could **exceed initial projections**]{.underline}.
Resembling a sinking ship, the [holes in the ideology of
non-proliferation as a result of Japan's potential proliferation would
**greatly** **outnumber** the states attempting to maintain
it]{.underline}. Once this symbolic ship has sunk, there would be
[**nothing preventing the complete ideological dissolution** of an often
underappreciated security measure, and the global geopolitical
environment may **enter** an era of **nuclear disarray**]{.underline}.

### Impact---Japan Prolif---Trade 

#### Japanese proliferation collapses [trade]{.underline}, the American and Japanese [economies]{.underline} and American [LNG exports]{.underline}.

**Acevedo et al '23** \[Ethan; 2023; Ethan is a Graduate of the Jackson
School of International Studies (a college within the University of
Washington Seattle) with a B.A. in Global and Regional Studies.Graduate
of the Jackson School of International Studies (a college within the
University of Washington Seattle) with a B.A. in Global and Regional
Studies; Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies, "Nuclear
Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation,"
https://jsis.washington.edu/southasia/wp-content/uploads/2022/10/23_TF-JSIS_495A_Pekkanen_Final-1.pdf;
accessed: 7.5.25\]

Japan-U.S. Economic Relationship

[**Japanese** nuclear **prolif**eration could compromise its essential
**trade** relationship with the United States, which would threaten its
economic autonomy and by extension, its security]{.underline}. The
Japanese economy is [highly dependent on American
consumption]{.underline} of its exports, particularly automobiles and
auto-parts[. Any **substantial decrease** in those exports could
severely hamper the Japanese economy since auto-related goods account
for **19.4% of Japan's total exports** while exports comprise **18% of
Japan's GDP**]{.underline} (Ministry of Finance of Japan 2023; OECD
2021). In 2021, the United States was Japan's second largest trade
partner after China while Japan was the United States' fifth largest
trade relationship respectively (JETRO 2021 (A)). Additionally, the
United States receives a massive 18% of Japan's total exports while
providing 10% of Japan's total imports. Although the United States is a
substantial export target for Japan, Japanese exports only accounted for
5% of U.S. imports and Japanese consumption of American goods was only
4% of American exports (Chanlett-Avery et al. 2023). The [United States
is one of the **primary consumers** of Japanese goods and its
consumption outpaces any reciprocal Japanese consumption of **American
goods**]{.underline}. Consequently, [American consumption comprises a
significant share of Japanese GDP and underscores how **dependent** the
Japanese economy is on the United States]{.underline}. Furthermore,
Japan accounts for a declining share of U.S. imports due to shifts in
the global supply chain and the transference of manufacturing to
developing countries like China (Chanlett-Avery et al. 2023). [Japanese
**economic dependency** on the United States is further cemented by the
fact that a large portion of U.S. imports to Japan are **l**iquefied
**n**atural **g**as, which Japan is heavily reliant on for its **energy
demands**]{.underline} (International Trade Administration, n.d.).
[Without U.S. oil imports, the Japanese economy would suffer immensely,
especially considering that the global oil supply is **unusually
volatile** as a consequence of the Russo-Ukrainian War]{.underline}.

While these figures show that Japan and the United States are
interdependent, it is clear that Japan --- with its smaller and stagnant
economy --- relies much more on the United States. Moving forward,
Japan's reliance on the United States will only continue to increase as
Japan's share of global exports decreases. Thus, while the [United
States would want to avoid a **total trade severance** with Japan, it
could **diversify** and rely on **other partners** if
necessary]{.underline}. Therefore, Japan [should not **proliferate**
because it would threaten its mutually **beneficial trade relationship**
with the United States]{.underline}.

Conclusion

If [Japan were to pursue **nuc**lear weapon**s**]{.underline}, it [could
**jeopardize** its critical **relationship** with the United
States]{.underline}, and ultimately create a situation where Japan is
less safe. In light of the extended nuclear umbrella's credible
deterrence, Japan should simply continue to engage in regular
communication with the United States and maintain updated guidelines for
security cooperation. Japan-U.S. bilateral security operations are the
most critical factor for sustaining the status quo in the Indo-Pacific
and preventing a revisionist state from taking unilateral military
action. [Consequently, Japanese proliferation would be totally
**antithetical** to maintaining the status quo because nuclear weapons
would radically change the real **power distribution** of the
Indo-Pacific]{.underline}. Furthermore, nuclear proliferation would be
an [enormous deviation from their **joint plans** and shared vision for
**regional security**]{.underline}. While Japan and the United States
have a good rapport, a shift of this degree would be [unquestionably
damaging to their relationship and would significantly undermine the
perception of their alliance]{.underline}. Additionally, any
deterioration in Japan-U.S. diplomatic relations is likely to [spillover
to their **economic relationship**]{.underline}, which would be
[**devastating** for the Japanese **economy**]{.underline}. To conclude,
Japan should adamantly avoid nuclear proliferation and instead pursue
ever-improving bilateral cooperation with the United States, its most
critical ally.

###  AT//Prolif Good---Senkaku 

#### No Senkakus impact.

**Xiaolin & Yufan '25** \[Duan and Hao; 2025; The Washington Quarterly,
"Surprising Stability in the Diaoyu/Senkaku Islands Since 2012," Vol.
48,
https://bpb-us-e1.wpmucdn.com/blogs.gwu.edu/dist/1/2181/files/2025/07/XiaolinYufan_TWQ_48_2.pdf;
accessed: 7.11.25\]

Recognizing the potential economic and geopolitical costs of such
combative diplomacy, [Chinese **leadership** has gradually moved to
moderate its **diplomatic tone**]{.underline}. In June 2021, Xi Jinping
[emphasized the importance of "adopting a **narrative tone** that
reflects openness and confidence, yet conveys modesty and
**humility**]{.underline}, in a bid to shape a reliable, admirable and
respectable image of China."35 The Wall Street Journal reported that
this shift in approach has been driven by Beijing's apprehension that
its confrontational "wolf-warrior" diplomacy may exacerbate anti-China
sentiment and potentially isolate the Chinese economy.36

Notably, outspoken and [proactive **diplomats** known for their
assertive **wolfwarrior style**]{.underline}, such as former deputy
spokesperson of the Chinese Foreign Ministry Zhao Lijian and former
ambassador to Sweden Gui Congyou, have [been **retired** or
reassigned]{.underline}. Since 2022, China has been actively seeking to
resume official and unofficial exchanges and trying to fix its broken
relations with Europe, Canada, Japan, Australia, and India, and maintain
stable working relations with the United States amid strategic
competition. Since November 2023, China has unilaterally granted
visa-free entry to citizens of forty-three countries including Germany,
France, Italy, Australia, and Japan.37 [This reflects a strategic effort
to boost people-to-people exchanges, revitalize post-COVID economic
engagement, and improve its global image amid rising geopolitical
tensions]{.underline}.

China's new charm offensive is targeting Japan as well. [Xi has
**repeatedly** stated his will to enhance China's **relations** with
**Japan**]{.underline} and met with Prime Minister Shinzo Abe ten times
from 2014 to 2019. At the 2019 Buenos Aires APEC Summit, Xi told
then-prime minister Abe that "China continues to welcome Japan to be
part of China's reform and opening up, sharing new opportunities of
China's development."38 As mentioned, China and Japan have engaged in
intensive minister-level meetings and social exchanges. Xi and
then-prime minister Kishida's meeting in November 2023 reaffirmed their
commitment to "the comprehensive promotion of a mutually beneficial
relationship based on common strategic interests."39

A [**major impetus** is both states' **willingness** to improve their
**economic ties**]{.underline}.40 [**Kishida** and **Xi** agreed to
establish a dialogue framework on **trade**, and their ministries in
charge discussed several key issues such as the stability of the
**supply chain**, **export control** of **semiconductor** manufacturing
equipment]{.underline}, China's entry into the Comprehensive and
Progressive Agreement for Trans-Pacific Partnership (CPTPP), and the
acceptance of new members into the RCEP (Regional Comprehensive Economic
Partnership).41 In January 2024, Chinese Premier Li Qiang met with
Japanese business leaders, underscoring the significant role of economic
and trade cooperation as "the ballast and propeller of bilateral
relations," two words echoing the previous language used to describe
China-US relations in China's official discourse prior to the two
countries' trade war.42 Additionally, China is active in promoting East
Asia integration, partly to offset the economic disengagement between
China and the United States. China, Japan, and Korea agreed to
accelerate free trade negotiations in March 2025---which had been on
pause since 2019 and were resumed in 2024---amid the growing concerns
over renewed tariff conflicts triggered by a second Trump
administration.

Unlike diversionary war theory, [**domestic challenges** have actually
generated **pacifying effects** over China's foreign
behavior]{.underline}. Conversely, Chinese leadership's perceptions of
favorable distribution of power in the international
system---particularly since the 2008 financial crises---have reinforced
a more assertive, albeit cautious, posture in the East China Sea and
South China Sea.43 As pointed out by Aaron Friedberg, "When Beijing
feels stronger it acts more assertively; when it feels weaker or more
vulnerable it acts with greater caution and restraint."44

Is the Diaoyu/Senkaku Dispute Still a Flashpoint?

The three factors discussed in the above sections play important roles
in pacifying the Diaoyu/Senkaku dispute from China's perspective,
creating incentives for Beijing to engage in strategic communication and
crisis management with Japan. These strategic choices---[restraint of
popular nationalist activism]{.underline}, [limited regional
aims]{.underline}, and [pursuit of **economic development** to quell
**domestic turmoil**]{.underline}---all [suggest]{.underline} that
[China has no intention to further alter the status quo]{.underline}.
Additionally, China's [routine navigation in disputed waters is
relatively transparent and predictable]{.underline}, [mitigating the
risks of misunderstandings and miscalculations]{.underline}. [Adding the
increasing **diplomatic engagements** between Beijing and Tokyo, the
**Diaoyu/Senkaku** dispute will not be a **flashpoint** in the
**foreseeable future**]{.underline}.

## AFF---Japan 

### NUQ---Alliance 

#### US Japan relations are deteriorating rapidly due to Trump's trade war and new stance on Japan, but there is still hope for a compromise.

**Ye Hee Lee and Tanaka '25** \[Michelle and Chie; July 12;Michelle Ye
Hee Lee is Tokyo bureau chief for the Washington Post, covering Japan
and the Koreas. Chie Tanaka is a writer for the Washington Post who
previously worked as a producer for TV Tokyo in New York and NHK in
Tokyo, with a focus on economic issues; The Washington Post, "Japan,
after 101 tough days, learns a hard lesson about US alliance,"
https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/2025/07/12/us-japan-alliance-trade-tensions/\]

It was only five months ago that President Donald Trump heralded the
"fantastic relationship" between the United States and Japan, as he sat
alongside Prime Minister Shigeru Ishiba, the first Asian leader to visit
him in his second stint in the White House. This week, I[shiba was again
first in line --- but not in a good way. He became the first foreign
leader to receive a scathing letter from the U.S. president, threatening
steep new tariffs if Tokyo didn't meet Trump's new deadline]{.underline}
for a trade deal. The letter stunned some officials in Japan, a security
ally for seven decades and a key partner in U.S. efforts to counter an
increasingly assertive China. But the deadlocked trade negotiations have
frustrated the American president --- who recently called Japan "so
spoiled." Now, Tokyo is learning that being one of Washington's best
friends doesn't carry much sway in Trump's second term, analysts say.
"[They're coming to a very hard realization that Japan is not special
enough to Trump," s]{.underline}aid Mireya Solís, director of the Center
for Asia Policy Studies at the Brookings Institution. "At the end of the
day, when Trump sees deficits, he's not thinking, 'This is my close
security partner.' He sees deficits." [It seemed like Japan was on good
footing when it became one of the first countries to begin negotiations,
in Apri]{.underline}l[. Washington wanted to strike a swift deal with
Tokyo to use as leverage against China,]{.underline} which runs its
biggest trade deficit, analysts say. Trump even showed up to the first
round of talks and gave Japan's trade negotiator Ryosei Akazawa a signed
red "Make America Great Again" cap, which Akazawa wore in the Oval
Office as he posed for a photo with two thumbs up. "A Great Honor to
have just met with the Japanese Delegation on Trade. Big Progress!" he
wrote on Truth Social after speaking with Akazawa. But there was not
much progress at all[. Japan did not readily give in to the Trump
administration's demands]{.underline}, not least because of the domestic
political considerations that constrained Ishiba's administration from
making concessions on key industries, such as autos, steel and rice.
Ishiba faces a tough parliamentary election on July 20 that could cost
the ruling party its majority in the upper house --- and Ishiba his
premiership. Japanese officials hoped Trump would carve out a
tariff-rate quota, like he did for some allies during his first term.
But the president quickly made it clear no one would be exempt this time
around --- not even Japan. "Japan is being treated the same as other
Asian nations, and that is not making policymakers here happy," said
Tokuko Shironitta, Japan country director at the Asia Group, a
consultancy. [As trade talks dragged on, Ishiba repeatedly stressed his
nation's unique standing as the largest foreign investor in the United
States since 2019]{.underline}, creating 1 million jobs. After all,
touting those investments had worked with Trump before. [This time, it
hasn't resonated. "It feels like we're still in a dense
fog,"]{.underline} Akazawa told reporters on June 10. They've now
completed seven rounds of talks. Hiroshi Oe, former chief trade
negotiator who worked on the Trans-Pacific Partnership deal with the
Obama administration, said [Trump is making "even more hard-line"
demands than expected. "Unfortunately, they no longer seem to operate
under the assumption that they should treat \[Japan\] with any special
consideration.]{.underline}" Japanese officials may have underestimated
Trump's deep-seated skepticism toward the country and relied too heavily
on good will, experts say. Some Japanese newspapers and prominent
thinkers are now calling on Japan to reconsider its tactics. "I believe
this was essentially a strategic mistake on the part of the Japanese
government and the Ishiba administration," said Kenji Minemura, senior
research fellow on Japanese foreign policy at the Canon Institute for
Global Studies, in Tokyo. "We're now in a completely different phase
from the first Trump administration. The U.S. wants different things
now." Some analysts suspect this shift may reflect how Trump has felt
about Japan all along. He developed an antipathy toward Japan when, as a
real estate developer in the 1980s, he witnessed Japan's rise to become
the world's second-largest economy and an existential threat to the
U.S.'s economic dominance. That was tempered in his first term, when
former prime minister Shinzo Abe forged a personal friendship with
Trump. Now, in the absence of Abe, who was assassinated in 2022, Tokyo
no longer has that bridge. "He's from the Japan-bashing generation,"
Minemura said. "That's why Trump's inherent suspicion of Japan has come
back to the surface." Trump's attacks on Japan don't seem to be letting
up anytime soon. In Tokyo, there are concerns that the allies are locked
in a trade fight when they should instead be banding together, given
security threats in the region. "When our relations are sour, or seem to
be sour from other countries' \[view\], I think that would only
encourage Russia, North Korea and China," said Ichiro Fujisaki, former
Japanese ambassador to the United States. Tokyo must find a way to
negotiate with Trump, even if a perfect "win-win" solution is not
possible. The resolution, Fujisaki said, may be one where "the U.S. win
is a capital-letter 'Win,' and our win is a small-letter 'win.' "Such a
resolution has proved elusive. Trump has been fixated on two matters
with Japan: Autos and rice. These are also the two areas where Tokyo
isn't willing to budge. "Japan really doesn't have that many concessions
to offer and the ones that it could offer, they're very reluctant to
grant," said Marcel Thieliant, Singapore-based head of Asia-Pacific
research at Capital Economics. Trump has repeatedly complained about the
lack of American-made cars entering Japan compared to the volume of
Japanese cars moving in the other direction. In 2024, Japan exported 1.4
million cars to the United States and imported just 16,074, Japanese
government figures show. Japanese auto experts say U.S. cars have long
been unpopular in Japan, because they are too big for its narrow roads
and parking spaces. The auto industry is the backbone of Japan's
economy, and negotiators have been unwilling to bend on car tariffs.
Japan may revisit auto safety standards that the U.S. says are making it
harder for its imports to enter Japan. But it has so far unsuccessfully
pushed the U.S. to roll back the 25 percent tariffs on cars and auto
parts. Rice is also a complicated issue for Japan, partly because of an
affection for farming, partly because of taste. The homegrown Japonica
grain --- known for its plump, round shape and natural sweetness --- has
been long cherished in Japan. It also becomes soft and sticky when
cooked, and is considered an ideal grain for traditional Japanese foods
such as sushi and rice balls. But for Trump, rice has come to symbolize
Japan's unfair trade barriers, experts say. "Rice is the easiest way for
him \[Trump\] to show that Japan has a closed market. It's symbolic,"
said Oe, the former Japanese negotiator, who previously handled
U.S.-Japan agricultural talks. Trump has indeed been hammering this
point home. "Japan, our friend, charges us 700 percent \[on rice\], but
that's because they don't want us selling rice and other \[products\],"
Trump said during a tariff announcement on April 2. Japanese officials
say that's not entirely accurate. Under a World Trade Organization
agreement, Japan has established a "minimum access" system for rice
imports, allowing up to 770,000 tons of rice to enter tax-free. The
United States is the biggest exporter of rice to Japan, and the vast
majority of it enters tax-free, trade statistics show. Another factor
helping open up Japan's rice market: A recent rice shortage that has
sent prices soaring had forced Japan to accept more imported grain.
Still, dramatically increasing imports could leave farmers vulnerable to
competition and draw the ire of the powerful agriculture lobby, making
it politically difficult for Ishiba to move from his stance that
protecting Japanese rice is a matter of "national interest," experts
say. But some say it may be time to reconsider that position, given the
rice crisis and the stalled trade talks. Because foreign rice is
cheaper, increasing imports can help more lower-income families as they
cope with higher prices --- and with proper messaging, voters may
understand, analysts say. "I believe it actually serves the national
interest" to accept more U.S. rice, Oe said. "[It doesn't undermine
Japan's position --- in fact, it can create a win-win outcome for both
Japan and its partners."]{.underline}

### NUQ---Alliance Unstable

#### Assurances and sentiment are low now---recent tariffs and pressure from China already causing Japanese uncertainty 

Christian **Caryl 7/17**, the former Moscow bureau chief for Newsweek
and U.S. News & World Report, "Why Japan Might Be America's Most
Frustrated Ally", *Foreign Policy*,
<https://foreignpolicy.com/2025/07/17/japan-us-trump-ishiba-election-tariffs-china-alliance-military-defense/?tpcc=recirc_latest062921>,
July 17, 2025

On July 7, U.S. President Donald [Trump]{.underline} sent a letter to
the Japanese government [announcing]{.underline} [a 25 percent tariff on
all goods shipped to the U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates
beginning on Aug. 1. The reaction was swift. "[The content is entirely
unacceptable,"]{.underline} said Itsunori Onodera, a senior member of
the ruling Liberal Democratic Party (LDP). "To notify a key ally with
nothing more than a single letter is extremely disrespectful, and I feel
a strong sense of indignation."

In a country where speaking one's mind so directly is generally frowned
on, this statement amounted to a shout of outrage. [The sense of
betrayal is palpable in Japan these days]{.underline}. Prime Minister
Shigeru [Ishiba thought that he had placated Trump's more extreme
demands]{.underline} by promising to invest an additional \$1 trillion
in the U.S. economy, eliciting praise from him at the time. The letter
from Washington came as a correspondingly harsh surprise. A poll
conducted in March and April found that 81 percent of Japanese felt
"more uneasy than hopeful" about Trump's second term in office; that
number is now bound to be much higher.

[Tokyo-watchers are]{.underline} now [trying to figure out whether the
perceived insult and broadside against the Japanese economy]{.underline}
from the White House [will hurt or help Ishiba]{.underline} in the upper
house election on July 20. The vote comes at a time when popular
discontent over inflation and rising unemployment is already spiking.
And that's not the only complicating factor: Tokyo is also struggling to
formulate a response to rising Chinese military assertiveness in an
already tense neighborhood. "[Typhoon Trump is wreaking havoc with
longstanding allies in Asia]{.underline}---Japan and South Korea---[in
ways that are undermining American power and influence in the
region]{.underline}," said Jeff Kingston, a professor at Temple
University in Tokyo. "His erratic diplomacy is [shredding]{.underline}
[trust in American reliability as an ally at a time when Tokyo is
feeling vulnerable to Beijing's hegemonic ambitions."]{.underline}

In the third week of June, [the Japanese watched with shock as China
sent]{.underline} not one but two of its [new aircraft carriers into the
Western Pacific.]{.underline} The ships [sailed through Japan's
exclusive economic zone]{.underline}, and one of them, the Liaoning,
crossed for the first time the so-called second island chain, a crucial
line of defense that connects Japan with the island of Guam, a major
U.S. military strongpoint. The [Chinese conducted drills]{.underline}
involving hundreds of takeoffs and landings of helicopters and fighter
planes, [and one of the jets buzzed a Japanese surveillance aircraft,
prompting an official protest from Tokyo.]{.underline}

On June 27, [the cabinet authorized the Japanese military to shoot down
Chinese drones that enter Japanese airspace]{.underline}---[an
uncharacteristically confrontational gesture that speaks volumes about
Japan's growing concern around Chinese incursions.]{.underline} Some
analysts have warned that Beijing could opt to call Tokyo's bluff by
flying a drone over Japanese territory just to trigger a reaction. The
[cabinet decision came just three days after the Japanese miliary
conducted its first missile test from within Japanese territory. The
weapon in question was a land-based anti-ship missile---another clear
signal to the Chinese]{.underline}.

Even under normal circumstances, [Chinese saber-rattling would have
provoked serious anxiety. But the circumstances right now are anything
but normal]{.underline}. The political elite in [Tokyo is harboring
doubts about its alliance with Washington that go **far beyond the
uncertainty** conjure]{.underline}d [up by earlier
disagreements]{.underline}. At a moment when the Japanese want to see
reassurance from their friends, they are instead being scolded as
"spoiled" by the U.S. president for resisting his tariff demands.

#### Recent canceled security talks after demands of concessions and tariffs prove alliance is unstable 

Christian **Caryl 7/17**, the former Moscow bureau chief for Newsweek
and U.S. News & World Report, "Why Japan Might Be America's Most
Frustrated Ally", *Foreign Policy*,

### NUQ---Trumpism 

#### Trump is killing the alliance 

**Pons '25** \[Philippe; June 26; Le Monde\'s correspondent in Japan; Le
Monde, "Japan is questioning its alliance with Donald Trump\'s United
States,"
https://www.lemonde.fr/en/opinion/article/2025/06/26/japan-is-questioning-its-alliance-with-donald-trump-s-united-states_6742727_23.html;
accessed: 7.18.25\]

But such **[assurances]{.underline}** have [not ruled out the
**unpredictability** of **Trump\'s decisions** −]{.underline} his
[tendency to view **alliances** as constraints]{.underline}, to conflate
economic and strategic interests and to [make demands that Tokyo
considers **unacceptable**]{.underline}. These demands included
requiring Japan to increase military spending from 2% of GDP (in 2027)
to 3.5%, or even 5%, to bring it in line with European levels. Japan is
realizing that it will be increasingly difficult to pursue a foreign
policy based on respect for international law and multilateralism.

[Japan]{.underline} diverged from the positions of Germany, France and
the United Kingdom by condemning Israel\'s attack on Iran, which it
judged \"unacceptable while negotiations are ongoing,\" Prime Minister
Shigeru Ishiba said on June 13. However, he was more circumspect
regarding the US strikes on Iranian nuclear sites on June 21, calling on
both sides to resume dialogue and later stating that he \"understood the
need\" to halt Iran\'s nuclear ambitions. \"We cannot openly say that we
disapprove of US\' actions,\" a source close to the Ministry of Foreign
Affairs told the daily Asahi.

Ishiba − who found himself isolated at the recent G7 summit − announced
that he would not attend, as an invited guest, the upcoming North
Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) summit in The Hague on June 24 and
25; the same is true for Australian Prime Minister Anthony Albanese and
South Korean President Lee Jae-myung. These absences reflect the waning
hopes of closer ties between NATO and its partners in the Asia-Pacific
region following the US strikes on Iranian nuclear sites.

[From Tokyo\'s perspective]{.underline}, \"The [United States is no
longer as **reliable**]{.underline} as it once was, and [developing an
**alternative diplomatic** and security policy]{.underline} to the one
pursued until now is essential in order [to reduce our
**dependence**]{.underline},\" said Professor Masayuki Tadokoro of the
International University of Japan. Indeed, [greater independence was
**desired** by **68%** of respondents]{.underline} in a poll conducted
by Asahi in May.

Much like the rest of the world, Japan has had to contend with Trump\'s
demands on tariffs (a baseline 10% imposed on most countries, with
additional surcharges of 25% on cars and 50% on steel and aluminum).
This was not unprecedented; in the 1970s and 1980s, the country had
already faced American demands punctuated by table-thumping.

Lack of trusted interlocutors

[Tokyo is locked in tough negotiations]{.underline}; it [**risks**
prompting Trump to once again raise the issue of the **alliance\'s
imbalance**]{.underline}. According to Trump, the US has committed to
defending Japan, but the reverse has not happened. This has not been
true since 2015, though, when Tokyo reinterpreted the pacifist
provisions of its Constitution in order to participate in collective
self-defense operations.

To demand a greater Japanese contribution to the cost of American bases
in the archipelago would be equally disingenuous, since Japan has
already covered 75%, or 211 billion yen (€1.3 billion). These naval, air
and land bases − which provided essential support to the US during the
Korean and Vietnam wars − currently forms its frontline in the Pacific.

There are limits to the leverage of protection. And yet, one question
has haunted Japanese leaders: Is the alliance with the US reliable? They
are increasingly unsure. After Joe Biden\'s election in 2022, political
scientist Toshihiro Nakayama of Keio University wrote: \"[Trump in power
or not, Trumpism is **here to stay**]{.underline}.\"

Indeed, \"Trump is the [**symptom of changes** in American society that
have not always been perceived in Japan]{.underline},\" wrote Robert
Dujarric of Temple University in Tokyo in the April issue of the Revue
internationale et stratégique, titled \"Le Japon et les métamorphoses de
la puissance\" (\"Japan and the Metamorphoses of Power\"), edited by
Marianne Péron-Doise.

The [**uncertainties** surrounding the alliance with the
US]{.underline}, [combined with violations of **international rules**
and the increased **use of force** in Europe and the Middle East -- and
perhaps tomorrow in Northeast Asia]{.underline} -- [has forced Japan to
confront \"the most **severe security environment** since the end of the
Second World Wa]{.underline}r,\" the prime minister said in his general
policy address in October 2024.

Tokyo has sought to play a greater role in the reshaping of global
geopolitics by actively pursuing \"a diplomacy of influence,\" in the
words of Péron-Doise. Centering on multilateralism, Tokyo is also
[remaining careful not to be drawn into an **anti-China \"crusade\"**
and to avoid being the **fall guy** in any \"deal\" struck between Trump
and Xi Jinping]{.underline}.

#### Trump crushes eager optimism between Japan and US. 

**Baylor & Smith \'25** \[Chris Baylor, Sheila A. Smith; Doctorate from
the University of California. Master\'s degree from Brown University;
7-17-2025, Expert on Japanese politics and foreign policy, Senior Fellow
for Asia-Pacific Studies, PhD degrees from the department of political

https://www.cfr.org/blog/us-japan-trade-impasse-ishiba-faces-another-election;
Accessed 7-18-2025; TS\]

But a weak political base is not Ishiba's only problem. The prime
minister also had to navigate the transition in U.S. leadership. During
his first term in office, Donald J. Trump and Shinzo Abe developed a
strong personal relationship, but [Abe's **death** meant that Ishiba had
to build his own relationship with Trump and his new
administration]{.underline}. On February 7, Ishiba traveled to
Washington, D.C., the second foreign leader to visit the Trump White
House. Outwardly, the visit seemed a success; the optics were good, and
the personal vibe was positive. Many Japanese observers believed that
the special relationship between Tokyo and Washington would continue.

[There were issues]{.underline}, to be sure, that promised
[**difficult** negotiations]{.underline} ahead. Trump remained wedded to
the idea that tariffs could be an effective weapon for recalibrating
allied ties. In addition, the fate of Nippon Steel's troubled effort to
acquire U.S. Steel remained unknown. Japanese businesses worried that
the Biden administration's decision to block the purchase by a Japanese
company due to "national security concerns" suggested a departure from
the close private-sector relationship between the two countries. Japan
was, after all, a U.S. ally and one of the largest sources of foreign
direct investment in the United States. This was made clear in the press
conference held by Ishiba and Trump after their meeting. So too was the
Japanese willingness to purchase more LNG from the United States, an
important energy priority for the Trump administration.

But the [early optimism of]{.underline} the [Ishiba-Trump meeting soon
began to fade]{.underline}. The [tariff announcement]{.underline} on
April 2 [changed the tone and tenor of U.S.-Japan talks]{.underline}.
[Trump imposed]{.underline} [a reciprocal tariff of 24 percent on Japan,
along with an additional 25 percent tariff on automobiles and their
parts, plus a 25 percent tariff on steel and aluminum. A **ninety-day
pause** was given on April 9 to allow countries to negotiate trade
deals]{.underline}, and the Japanese government got to work on crafting
a bilateral deal.

Trump's proposed [tariffs]{.underline} would be [**disastrous** for
Japanese businesses]{.underline}. The Japan Business Federation, or
Keidanren, expressed serious concerns, noting that blanket tariffs could
have "[a serious impact]{.underline} not only [on Japan-U.S. economic
relations]{.underline} but also on the global economy and the free trade
system." Japan's auto industry would suffer the most. The Japan
Automobile Manufacturers Association (JAMA) stated that Japanese
automakers have made significant contributions to the U.S. economy and
that mutual trust should be preserved. Estimates from Toyota and Honda
revealed stunning profit losses---for the Japanese fiscal year 2026,
Toyota forecasts a [35 percent profit **loss**]{.underline}**,** while
Honda forecasts a 70 percent loss.

#### Trade talks have been [fruitless]{.underline}. 

**Tang \'25** \[Francis Tang; News Reporter at The Japan Times covering
Ministry of Economy, Trade and Industry, Ministry of Justice and
Immigration Services Agency, foreign relations and businesses;
7-16-2025; \"Trump signals the end of the road for Japan tariff

U.S. President Donald [Trump expressed **frustration** with
Japan]{.underline} [over trade]{.underline} and said that its
[\"reciprocal\" tariff rate of 25%,]{.underline} mentioned in a letter

to go into effect on Aug. 1.

"The letters are a deal," [Trump]{.underline} told reporters on Tuesday
at Joint Base Andrews in Maryland, with U.S. Commerce Secretary Howard

"I will [veer from]{.underline} those [deals on]{.underline} occasion

"So, we might just stick with a letter."

Trump suggested in his comments that South Korea might be close to

Additional tariffs on pharmaceutical products might be imposed as soon
as the end of July, Trump added while speaking to reporters on the

On Wednesday morning in Tokyo, Deputy Chief Cabinet Secretary Kazuhiko
Aoki declined to comment on Trump's remarks.

The U.S. president sent letters to dozens of countries notifying them of
the r[eciprocal tariff rate to be imposed on their goods]{.underline}
from Aug. 1. These rates will apply to almost all exports to the United
States and replace a 10% baseline rate now being charged.

Reciprocal tariffs will not be charged on goods subject to separate
sector-specific tariffs, which include a 25% duty on vehicles and auto
parts and a 50% duty on steel and aluminum.

[Tariff talks between the United States and Japan have been intense but

apart**]{.underline}.

already imposed as a precondition for discussions and the United States
insisting that only the reciprocal tariffs were on the agenda.

has said that this remains its top priority.

[and]{.underline} that it has [refused to buy American
