### Link Turn---Science Diplomacy 

#### They like the plan.

**Arguello & Rafaly '23** \[Gabriela and Vonitsoa; December 18; Gabriela
Argüello, BA, LLM, LLD, is an Associate Senior Lecturer at the Law
Department, Vonintsoa Rafaly is a Research Fellow at the University of
Copenhagen (Denmark). After completing her JD in International law of
the sea at the University of Nantes; Polar Record, "Science diplomacy
and Asian states: Transforming the governance landscape in the Arctic,"
Vol. 59; accessed: 7.18.25\]

While binding agreements may still foster cooperation in the Arctic,
productive collaboration in soft-law forums like the Arctic Council is
uncertain. The war in Ukraine disrupted the trust-building effort in
Arctic governance and left the Russian scientific community isolated
(Andreeva, Reference Andreeva2023; Zaika & Lagutina, Reference Zaika and
Lagutina2023). This isolation effectively disrupts the scientific
networks developed under the auspices of the Arctic Council, a forum
"that intertwines knowledge creation and decision-making" (Andreeva,
Reference Andreeva2023). As a result, any efforts to resume scientific
cooperation will require a mediator who is trusted, credible and
acceptable to all Arctic states. (Bisen, Reference Bisen2023). Arguably,
Asian states can serve as a mediator between the West and Russia.
Science diplomacy is a field where Asian states can intensify
cooperation ties with the West while still involving Russia (Berkman,
Baeseman & Shibata, Reference Berkman, Baeseman and Shibata2022).
[**Japan**, for instance, places **great importance** on **research**
and **technology** in shaping the **Arctic** legal framework and
fostering collaboration in the region]{.underline}. This state [has not
precluded the **possibility** of continuing its **collaboration** with
both **Western states** and **Russia**]{.underline} (Hataya, Reference
Hataya2023).

China's political influence on the world stage and involvement in Arctic
science make it a potential mediator between the West and Russia,
according to certain commentators (Chen, Reference Chen2023). It is
noteworthy that China still maintains official contact with all Arctic
states, including Russia. More importantly, it could use its role as an
observer in the Arctic Council to promote collaboration between Russia
and other Arctic and non-Arctic states (Chen, Reference Chen2023).
Additionally, G20 and the Indian presidency are also acting as mediators
(Bisen, Reference Bisen2023). The G20 Forum remains one of the limited
platforms where cooperation between the West and Russia continues. In
December 2022, India took over from Indonesia to assume the Presidency
of the G20 Forum (G20, 2022). During its presidency, climate change is a
priority for the organisation and since the Arctic plays a fundamental
role in the regulation of the earth's climate, G20 could work towards
normalising scientific cooperation in the Arctic (Bisen, Reference
Bisen2023).

Moreover, Norway assumed the chairmanship of the Arctic Council for the
period 2023--2025, effectively replacing Russia. At the 13th Arctic
Council Meeting in May 2023, the Arctic Eight, including Russia, and six
permanent participants declared their commitment to promoting
cooperation and stability in the region through the work of the Arctic
Council. (Arctic Council, 2023). Although Russia is committed to being
active in this forum, Nikolay Korchunov, the Russian Senior Arctic
Official, stated that the work of the Council must be fully restored and
all projects must be beneficial to the interests of all Arctic states
(Jonassen, Reference Jonassen2023). At this stage, it is important not
to overlook the role of observers, including Asian states, in the
process of normalising activities within the Arctic Council. However,
despite the promises that a new chairmanship may bring to the Arctic
Council, many still raise concerns about the potential fragmentation of
Arctic governance. As we discuss in the following section, there is
still a possibility that Russia will strengthen its cooperation efforts
with Asian states while dismissing cooperative efforts with the West.

A disruptive effect on the balance of power

Prior to the Ukraine crisis, Arctic governance was based on the soft
power of science. The high degree of cooperation and collaboration in
science, technology and innovation projects helped to maintain
geopolitical stability. Nowadays, the inability to maintain
long-standing cooperation within the Arctic Council and other regional
bodies is likely to create disruption in the institutional order in the
Arctic (Caymaz, Reference Caymaz2023; Exner-Pirot & Murray, Reference
Exner-Pirot and Murray2017). From a governance perspective, studies
envision several directions among which the development of pan-Asian
Arctic era, or towards a Western Arctic Council and Russia/Asia
coalition (Koivurova & Shibata, Reference Koivurova and Shibata2023,
p.8; Landriault & Minard, Reference Landriault and Minard2022). In both
cases, dismantling epistemic knowledge can politicise Arctic governance,
endangering the scientific integrity of the Arctic Council.

First, regarding Asian states' ramp-up presence in the Arctic, and their
role in Arctic research, this interim period is likely to promote a new
Arctic Asian era, particularly as Russia's relations with Western
nations continue to deteriorate. A case in point is the withdrawal of
Russia from BEAC, where the Ministry of Foreign Affairs of Russia
characterised the suspension of activities in this forum as the "fault"
of Western member states. The withdrawal statement opens the possibility
to continue collaboration in Arctic affairs with other partners (The
Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the Russian Federation, 2023). These
partners may well include Asian states.

The suspension of the Arctic Council's operations is also negatively
impacting the availability of accurate and comprehensive scientific data
on the Arctic for decision-makers, at the international, regional and
national levels (Johnstone, Reference Johnstone2015). For instance, the
isolation of Russia will close access to one of the largest Arctic
maritime zones for scientific purposes. It will be detrimental to the
production of regular and comprehensive assessments of the Arctic
environmental status. The dismantlement of activities in research,
science and innovation will lead, among others, to the fragmentation of
knowledge, a cost increase in scientific production, more pronounced
competition between Arctic and non-Arctic states, and fragmented effort
in addressing environmental issues in the Arctic. Asian states hold
critical infrastructure and knowledge in advancing Arctic science, and
their effort in investing in Arctic projects is significant. For
instance, Japan and South Korea launched the construction of an
icebreaker for scientific purposes, to be deployed in 2026 (Lindgren &
Lanteigne, Reference Lindgren and Lanteigne2023). On the other side, the
development of pan-Asian cooperation over Arctic issues reveals their
willingness to have a leading role in Arctic affairs. This is the case
of the Asian Forum for Polar Sciences (AFoPS), the only scientific
cooperative organisation in Asia, created in 2004 to encourage and
facilitate cooperation to advance polar science among countries in the
Asian region (Kim & Jeong, Reference Kim and Jeong2015). In relation to
scientific research areas, a trilateral cooperation between China, Japan
and South Korea was launched in 2016, concerning mutual coordination,
data sharing and collaborative surveys (Danner, Reference Danner2020;
Valery, Reference Valery2018). Other conferences enabling informal
dialogues give momentum for a greater involvement of Asian states in the
Arctic, at a time of conflict. [The **last Arctic Circle Forum** held in
**Japan**]{.underline}, on 4--6 March 2023 reinforced Asian states'
willingness to be perceived as an epicentre for polar research and
dialogue in the future of the Arctic (Lindgren & Lanteigne, Reference
Lindgren and Lanteigne2023). These cooperative frameworks [**assert**
and **enhance** the presence of **Asian states** in the **Arctic**
debate, with a risk of "Easternizing" the Arctic]{.underline}.

Second, Asian states seem to be divided in their ties and ally with the
Arctic Eight. While the Ukraine crisis gave momentum for better
opportunities for China and India's cooperation with Russia, it opened
the way for closer collaboration between the other Arctic states and
Japan, South Korea on economic, scientific and security matters (Bloom,
Reference Bloom2022). The actual trend is that scientific cooperation
with Russia is riskier -- both for Arctic and non-Arctic states. As a
protest against the invasion of Ukraine, scientific events involving
Russia may face boycotts. Such practices are likely to hinder the
epistemic community's cooperation established around the Arctic
environmental research and/or promote a "Western Arctic" (Raspotnik,
Reference Raspotnik2018). One scenario would be the possibility of a
closer cooperation between Russia and Asian states -- especially China
and India, while becoming estranged from the West (Koivurova &
Lanteigne, Reference Koivurova and Lanteigne2022). Russia may increase
cooperation with China and India to strengthen existing political and
economic alliances. For instance, Russia and China recently renewed
their commitment for a long-standing cooperation (Jinping, Reference
Jinping2023). Yet, the assumption of closer ties between Russia and
Asian states cannot be taken for granted. China has not only remained
neutral in the Russia--Ukraine war, but apparently, Sinopec (a Chinese
state-run company) announced the suspension of petrochemical and gas
activities in Russia (Aizhu, Zhu & Xu, Reference Aizhu, Zhu and Xu2022).
Chinese commentators qualify Russian collaboration as opportunistic,
since Russia had a more distant relationship with China before the
annexation of Crimea, in 2014, especially regarding scientific research
in the Russian part of the Arctic Ocean (Sun, Reference Sun2018). From
the Russian local level, China's investment in science, technology and
innovation has long been viewed with scepticism, regarding its impact on
domestic companies, the environment and the threat to regional security
(Zhang & Xu, Reference Zhang and Xu2020).

Therefore, while [**Asian states** can act as a **bridge** between the
**West** and **Russia** to maintain **manageable cooperation**, for
scientific and non-scientific matters]{.underline}, it appears that it
cannot be taken for granted due to the fragile balance of power at the
time of the Ukrainian crisis.

### Troops Check 

#### Troops provide secure assurances 

Alexander **Lanoszka 19**, assistant professor of international
relations at the University of Waterloo and an honorary fellow at City,
University of London, 6-11-2019, \"False Alarm? Donald Trump, Alliances
and Nuclear Proliferation,\" East West,
https://www.eastwest.ngo/idea/false-alarm-donald-trump-alliances-and-nuclear-proliferation

[Some observers argue the prospects for nuclear proliferation among U.S.
allies appear to have heightened in recent years]{.underline}.
Superficially, U.S. President Donald Trump may be responsible. During
his 2016 presidential campaign, he made statements suggesting he might
tolerate efforts by Japan and South Korea to acquire nuclear weapons.
His criticisms of NATO and some of its members have fueled debate in
Germany on the desirability and feasibility of the "nuclear option."
Looking more broadly, since the 2008 financial crisis, the U.S. has had
to grapple with the management of its security ties abroad amid mounting
budgetary deficits and growing public weariness of so-called "forever
wars." [And yet, over [two years into]{.mark} the [Trump]{.mark}
administration, [little]{.mark} nuclear [prolif]{.mark}eration-related
activity [seems to be taking place]{.mark} among U.S.
allies]{.underline}. In [fact, some of the most powerful U.S.
allies---Japan and Germany---have largely moved away from nuclear
energy]{.underline}. What lies behind this current scenario? **[Perhaps
[one answer is that the U.S military deployments that underscore]{.mark}
U.S. security [commitments remain strong]{.mark}]{.underline}**. But how
do security guarantees relate to nuclear proliferation? Presidential
rhetoric may be important for evaluating the strength of a treaty pledge
by the U.S. to defend an ally in the event of attack by a potential
adversary like Russia, North Korea or China. However, whether it is more
important than other considerations is debatable, since presidential
rhetoric may simply be just that: rhetoric. A verbally cited promise to
follow through on any pledges recorded in print is simply hard to
believe. No country wants to risk the safety of his or her country's
cities for an ally, if they can help it. How can a state, therefore,
have any sort of confidence in its alliances? Thankfully, there is a
way. [The U.S. deploys a wide range of military forces to shore up local
defense and deterrence measures to augment the security of its allies
and to advance its own national security interests]{.underline}. U.S.
forces stationed in South Korea and Japan, for example, are there for
regional contingency if North Korea and/or China were to commit an act
of aggression---not to serve as potential targets to trigger wider
retaliation by Washington, per the logic of trip-wires. They are also
there to kill, making it hard for any potential aggressor to go
successfully on the attack without incurring unacceptable costs. [**[So
long as the U.S. has]{.mark} such [forces on its ally's
territory]{.mark}, or in the theater of operations where a potential war
would be fought, [the]{.mark} security [guarantee is
sufficient]{.mark}ly strong [to have]{.mark} the [confidence of its
beneficiaries.]{.mark}** It is when the U.S. has unilaterally reduced
its forward deployments that its allies become so alarmed they are
tempted to acquire nuclear weapons to ensure their own security. A case
in point is South Korea in the 1970s.]{.underline} As it does now, South
Korea benefited from a treaty promising that Washington would defend it
against external aggression. But when President Richard Nixon came into
office in 1969, he spoke of how partners in East Asia and elsewhere
would need to bear more of the collective defense burden, especially in
light of its failures in Vietnam, rising anti-war sentiment at home and
the downturn in the U.S. economy. Yet, the South Korean President at the
time---Park Chung-hee---was unfazed by Nixon's rhetoric. He believed
that the United States would reward his country for its contributions in
the Vietnam War by retaining its large military presence on the Korean
Peninsula. President Park's optimism was misplaced. In July 1970, Nixon
announced that the U.S. would unilaterally withdraw 20,000 of its
military personnel from South Korea within a year, cutting the total
U.S. military presence on the peninsula by one-third. The balance of
power implications were potentially significant. Since more troop
reductions were now conceivable, South Korea would become newly
vulnerable to North Korea---an adversary that, at the time, was
relatively more industrialized, enjoyed Chinese and Soviet patronage,
had a stronger conventional military and had engaged in highly
provocative but low-level military activities across the Demilitarized
Zone against the South since 1966. Against this backdrop, President Park
put South Korea on the path towards acquiring nuclear weapons. This
project failed partly because South Korea needed outside sources for
credit and access to reprocessing and enrichment facilities---a
vulnerability that the United States used to its advantage to get its
ally to curb its activities and to make nonproliferation commitments.
Still, South Korea has had persistent interest in fissile materials
since the 1970s and continues to undertake continued pyroprocessing
activities. Today, South Korea may not be on an aggressive path to
acquiring nuclear weapons, but its apparent desire to seek some
advantage demonstrates that security guarantees---once broken---can be
very difficult to repair, thus prompting countries to reconsider the
nuclear option. The South Korean case illuminates why we are seeing no
new efforts to get nuclear weapons, at least by U.S. treaty allies, in
the age of Trump. When one looks beyond the rhetoric, actions speak
volumes. The Trump administration has reinforced U.S. force posture with
additional deployments to Germany. It is seriously considering a
permanent military presence in neighboring Poland. Present relations
with South Korea have been rocky, but the Trump administration did sign
an updated free trade agreement, as well as a temporary agreement, to
help cover the costs of 28,500 military personnel stationed there. The
U.S. military presence in Japan has remained steady. Indeed, if Trump is
serious about containing China, the U.S. military presence will likely
increase in East Asia over the long-term. [Dramatic, unilateral troop
withdrawals under the Trump administration are still possible, but the
historical record indicates that such attempts are generally made early
in a presidency rather than later. Nixon, for instance, announced his
troop withdrawal out of Vietnam within eighteen months of coming into
office. **Putting all rhetoric aside, [if the military
foundations]{.mark} of U.S. security guarantees [remain strong]{.mark},
nuclear [prolif]{.mark}eration [among allies remains
unlikely.]{.mark}**]{.underline}

#### Troops maintain security guarantees 

**Hunzeker and Lanoszka 16** -- Michael Allen Hunzeker is an assistant
professor at George Mason University's School of Public, Government and

http://www.alexlanoszka.com/HunzekerLanoszkaParameters.pdf)//RP

How **[Landpower]{.underline}** Helps **[Generate
Credibility]{.underline}** Landpower---particularly [[forward-deployed
**landpower**]{.underline}]{.mark}---[[helps]{.mark} American [security
guarantees appear]{.mark} more [credible]{.mark}]{.underline}. It shapes
how other states perceive America's willingness and ability to implement
its promises and threats. To clarify, [the benefits of
landpower]{.underline} we describe below [exist when the overriding
political objective is to defend an ally from external
aggression]{.underline}. These benefits might not exist if the goal is
to support a domestically unpopular regime face its internal enemies. In
such cases, landpower could become a liability if its presence can
provoke resentment from the local population and become a target for
counterinsurgency. American Willingness [Strategists and international
security scholars have]{.underline} long [understood that putting ground
troops on an ally's territory]{.underline} [is one of the most effective
ways the U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates [can **make itself
look like a willing part- ner**]{.underline}.20 [Forward-based
[troops]{.mark}]{.underline} deployed in areas of vital interest do
three things. First, they [[are a **tangible indicator** of]{.mark}
American [willingness to fight]{.mark}.]{.underline} [Allies and
adversaries can observe troop deployments and track troop
levels]{.underline}. Ground troops are also expensive to garrison
overseas. That the United States bears many of these costs offers
further evidence it is serious about its commitments. Second,
forward-based ground [troops demonstrate the United States has "skin in
the game." They [soothe fears the]{.mark} [U]{.mark}]{.underline}nited
[[S]{.underline}]{.mark}tates [[will abandon its ally]{.mark} in a
crisis **[by intertwining]{.mark}** American **[lives with allied
interests]{.mark}**.]{.underline} When ground troops are on allied soil,
even a small conflict where key American interests are at stake could
kill Americans. Allies and adversaries know the loss of American life
will trigger calls for retribution, making it hard for American leaders
to retreat. Accordingly, [strategists argue ground
[troops]{.mark}]{.underline} are one of the most important ways the
United States can [[make]{.mark} its promise to use **[nuclear weapons
more credible]{.mark}**]{.underline}.21 Third, basing troops overseas
also makes it more difficult to stay neutral in a crisis because ground
troops are not easy to withdraw. Ships and aircraft can sail and fly
away on short notice, but it takes considerable time and money to
re-deploy thousands of ground troops. More importantly, extracting
ground troops during a crisis entails serious reputational costs. Thomas
Schelling captured this logic when discussing American troops in West
Berlin: ..\[t\]he reason we got committed to Berlin, and stayed
committed, is that if we let the Soviets scare us out of Berlin we would
lose face with the Soviet (and communist Chinese) leaders. It would be
bad enough to have Europeans, Latin Americans, and Asians think that we
are immoral or cowardly. It would be far worse to lose our reputation
with the Soviets.22 [Recent scholarship supports these arguments,
demonstrating that troop [deployments **discourage**]{.mark} the
**[allies]{.mark}** who host them **[from acquiring nuclear
weapons.]{.mark}**]{.underline}23 Indeed, some states have responded to
troop withdrawals by trying to acquire nuclear weapons.24 Historians
have shown South Korea began its nuclear program shortly after President
Richard Nixon withdrew the 7th US Infantry Division from the Korean
peninsula.25 Even West Germany, amid indications the United States was
going to reduce its military presence in Western Europe, entered into a
trilateral initiative with France and Italy to develop nuclear
weapons.26 In both cases, troop redeployments signaled the United States
was no longer heeding the security interests of those allies who still
confronted more powerful adversaries.

### No Prolif---Japan 

#### Lack of assurances won't drive Japan to proliferate

Domestic Stability and Elite Consensus on Foreign Policy

Japan was domestically stable in this period and experienced rapid
economic growth, as discussed in Chapter 4. The country also had
moderately high levels of elite consensus on foreign policy. Japan was a
multiparty democracy dominated by the conservative Liberal Democratic
Party (LDP). [Although defense officials and officers sometimes had
different views from those of civilians, the government had **strict
civilian control** of the country's defense forces]{.underline}. The
mainstream faction within the LDP wanted to remain in power by
respecting the preferences of the pacifist, antimilitarist public and
continuing to deliver economic growth.677 [Increasing defense spending,
in its view, would be a drag on growth]{.underline}. The LDP also
believed that [militarization would **worsen Japan's relations** with
countries in the region and revive the Japanese defense forces as a
political force]{.underline}. The LDP therefore preferred to continue to
[**rely on the U.S.-Japan security** relationship because it was a more
cost-effective way to meet Japan's security needs and maintain a world
order that benefited Japan]{.underline}. This conservative mainstream
faction of the LDP remained in control of foreign policy. [It was
challenged from the right by nationalist factions within the LDP who
found Japan's dependence on the United States demeaning and wanted Japan
to **aggressively rearm**, and it was challenged from the left by
opposition parties who feared entrapment by the United States and wanted
Japan to **disarm** and abrogate the security treaty]{.underline}.678 As
a result, there was some modest, politically relevant discord on foreign
policy that could have affected Japan's response to U.S. retrenchment.

Availability of Alternative Partners

[Japan had poor prospects for finding alternative allies to the United
States]{.underline} for several reasons. Japan was constitutionally
prohibited from defending any other country. Furthermore, [Japan's
wartime atrocities]{.underline} made it more difficult to deepen ties
with South Korea or other regional states (see Chapter 5).679 Japan also
refrained from participating in regional security institutions so as not
to alarm regional countries---including Southeast Asian countries---that
strongly remembered Japan's atrocities in the Pacific War.680

Implications for Japan's Response to U.S. Retrenchment

The [moderate level of U.S. retrenchment and **rapprochement with
Japan's rivals would not have led to a major change** in Japan's
vulnerability]{.underline}, [creating **only weak incentives** to settle
differences with its rivals or devote more resources toward
balancing]{.underline} (compared with a more insecure state, such as
South Korea). [Pacifist and pro-growth public attitudes would have
**made it hard** for Japan's leaders to devote significantly more
resources toward building up its conventional forces, **even if they
wanted to**. Japan's constitutional limitations, wartime behavior, and
lack of regional security institutions made it less likely that Japan
**would devote more resources toward external balancing
instead**]{.underline}.

### No Prolif---Japan---Laundry List

#### Laundry list of reasons as to why Japan can't and won't proliferate

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional
Studies from the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies; Amy
Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of
Washington; Roger Clemens, B. A. in Political Economy and Global and
Regional Studies from the University of Washington; Claire Paiemont, B.
A. in International Studies and Economies from the University of
Washington; Kay Reed, B. A. in Global and Regional Studies from the
University of Washington; The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program @
the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies @ University of
Washington, \"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation\",
2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

With [consideration for the **serious** **ramifications** of nuclear
proliferation]{.underline} --- which include [harming **key diplomatic**
**and economic** partners, **igniting conflict** with foreign
adversaries]{.underline}, [**hindering** one's own **economy** and
energy supply]{.underline}, originating a sudden [rise of
**international proliferation**]{.underline}, and [provoking domestic
and international scrutiny that sows suspicion over the **ethics and
priorities** of the country]{.underline} --- [any effort to proliferate
from Japan has the probability of **creating** a **more dangerous**
situation]{.underline} [than proliferation would **ever possibly
solve**]{.underline}.

One current international objective of the United Nations is the
reduction in use and eventual elimination of nuclear weapons as evident
by the Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons (NPT) from
1970 and the Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons (TPNW) from
2021. In part, this objective is highlighted by the United Nations with
such frequency because the dangerous nature of nuclear weapons --- which
is not unknown to Japan, the first and only country to suffer the direct
and brutal impacts of nuclear bombings --- goes beyond the casualties
and infrastructural damage and instead extends to even their creation
and storage, which are risky undertakings on their own. The
[implications of proliferating]{.underline} and obtaining weaponry of
this caliber [are clear]{.underline}: [possession of the power to cause
mass casualties and infrastructural damage to cities and the intent and
comfortability with using them. For Japan, such an **extreme**
**reversal** in its conduct on the international stage would undoubtedly
**cause a global disturbance**]{.underline}.

In acknowledgement of arguments for the proliferation of Japan, we
recognize the [existence of foreign nations frequently regarded as
threats to Japan]{.underline} -- often due to [**territorial disputes**
such as the Senkaku Islands (尖閣諸島]{.underline}) [with]{.underline}
the People's Republic of China (PRC, "[China]{.underline}")
[and]{.underline} the Republic of China (ROC, "[Taiwan]{.underline}")
[or the **Northern Territories** (北方領土)]{.underline}
[with]{.underline} the Russian Federation ("[Russia]{.underline}").
[Additional threats include]{.underline} the Democratic People's
Republic of Korea (DPRK, "[North Korea]{.underline}") and its [usage of
**intimidation tactics** such as missile testing in the Sea of Japan and
North Pacific Ocean]{.underline} -- waters surrounding not only Japan
but its ally, the Republic of Korea (ROK, "South Korea").

Fortunately, the current [international environment]{.underline} at this
time [**does not legitimize** the nuclear proliferation of
Japan]{.underline} when [**weighed against the credibility of foreign
threats** and the overall significance of these conflicts]{.underline}
as discussed in further depth in Chapter I Section II--V. In studying
these foreign conflicts, our findings indicate that they [do not
constitute the **escalation** of national security initiatives to the
extent of proliferation, especially in the instance of territorial
conflicts]{.underline}. The [probability of proliferation
**aggravating** these current **threats** is immense]{.underline}, and
therefore, proliferating would be [**contradictory** to Japan's
objectives of establishing stability and democracy in East
Asia]{.underline}.

In Chapter I Section I, we assert the notion that the U.S.-Japan
Security Treaty and subsequent alliance of forces are sufficient for the
prolonged defense of Japan. In conjunction with the Japanese
Self-Defense Forces (JSDF), the American military can adequately address
the current international and regional threat environment and any future
adjustments to those environments. Additionally, the American nuclear
umbrella is robust enough to defend Japan should it require the usage of
nuclear weapons, creating a situation where Japanese proliferation would
be unnecessary in light of the other consequences facing Japan should it
proliferate.

To further address the non-diplomatic consequences of Japanese
proliferation, Chapter II discusses the [economic consequences of
proliferation domestically and internationally]{.underline} as well as
the opportunity costs of proliferation. We emphasize the [probability of
**sanctions**]{.underline} --- a particularly [challenging obstacle for
Japan]{.underline}, who is [heavily **reliant on the importation** of
natural resources and other materials]{.underline} --- and [decreased
foreign investment opportunities]{.underline} and the subsequent
repercussions these consequences would have on the Japanese economy.
This [establishes that Japanese proliferation is]{.underline} not only
[**dangerous** for foreign diplomacy and security purposes]{.underline}
but is [difficult due to the opportunity cost to its nuclear energy
supply and]{.underline} the [potential sanctions]{.underline} on
importation of other energy sources such as petroleum.

In an effort to acknowledge the international ramifications of Japan
proliferating from a non-diplomatic standpoint, Chapter III details the
"**[domino effect]{.underline}**," where [one country's proliferation
results in a chain reaction of several other countries']{.underline}
[subsequent proliferation]{.underline}. This would
[jeopardize]{.underline} not only [Japan's professed claim of increasing
non-proliferation movements but also the United Nations' efforts to
increase non-proliferation and disarmament internationally]{.underline}.
This would [likely have the **adverse effect** of causing mass
proliferation worldwide]{.underline} as **[even the most
pacifist]{.underline}** and [anti-nuclear societies **obtain nuclear
weapons**]{.underline}. This would pose a much great[er risk to Japan
than the initially projected regional threats of China, North Korea, and
Russia and would **damage regional and global security**]{.underline}.

However, [if Japan chooses to ignore the previous concerns]{.underline},
Chapter IV addresses the prominence of the Japanese anti-nuclear
identity and the **[domestic barriers]{.underline}** the [Japanese
government would encounter when trying to proliferate]{.underline}.
Section I acknowledges [Japanese society's lengthy history with nuclear
accidents]{.underline}, highlighting the [atomic bombings of Hiroshima
and Nagasaki]{.underline}, the Daigo Fukuryu Maru incident, and the
[Fukushima Nuclear Disaster]{.underline} as they [relate to a **growing
anti-nuclear sentiment** among the Japanese public]{.underline}. It
further serves to assess the [contradictory relationship between the
Japanese public and nuclear energy]{.underline}, [negating the argument
that public support for nuclear energy indicates public support for
proliferation]{.underline}.

Section II outlines the current interpretations of [Article
9]{.underline} [and the Three Non-Nuclear Principles]{.underline},
including their [restrictions and the validity of certain claims put
forth by the Japanese government]{.underline} --- most specifically that
there is a [capacity for so-called "**defensive nuclear
weapons**]{.underline}" under Article 9, which we argue **[there is
not]{.underline}**. We further detail the subsequent challenges the
Japanese government would face in proliferating due to the [Japanese
**public's current interest in maintaining Article 9** and the Three
Non-Nuclear Principles in their current state and the national
referendum requirement necessary for the formal amendment
process]{.underline}. Therefore, we suggest that an [attempt at
proliferation is **unlikely if it is fully democratic and
constitutional**]{.underline}.

#### [Sanctions]{.underline}, [preemption]{.underline}, AND [US backlash]{.underline}.

[Another factor working against]{.underline} **[Japanese]{.underline}**

[decision]{.underline} by either country [to]{.underline} **[withdraw
from the NPT]{.underline}** [and pursue]{.underline}
**[nuc]{.underline}**lear weapon**[s]{.underline}** development [would
be a]{.underline} **[body blow]{.underline}** [to the global
nonproliferation regime and]{.underline} **[elicit strong international

expected to **[scale back]{.underline}** [cooperation in certain

[which]{.underline} **[depend]{.underline}** [on nuclear power for

**[national]{.underline}** [nuclear forces and]{.underline} their

**[potential targets of preemptive attack]{.underline}** in a crisis,
[especially in the]{.underline} **[early]{.underline}** [stages of

In weighing the costs and benefits of going nuclear, [a]{.underline}

#### Japan will never nuclearize\-\--requires US approval, testing, and full US withdrawal of the Nuclear Umbrella

**Ghazleh 22** \[Mohammad Abu Ghazleh, Senior Researcher at TRENDS
Research & Advisory, Abu Dhabi, UAE, Assistant Professor of Political
Science at the Arab Academy in Denmark, adjunct professor at the United
Arab Emirates University, Abu Dhabi \"Five reasons why Japan will
unlikely go nuclear\", May 14, 2022,
https://jordantimes.com/opinion/mohammad-abu-ghazleh/five-reasons-why-japan-will-unlikely-go-nuclear,
accessed 7-18-2025\] /ety

The [Russian invasion to Ukraine raised, perhaps more than any other
time, the question as to whether Japan **should or should not go
nuclear**]{.underline}. On March 25, former prime minster Shinzo Abe
shocked Japan, and the surrounding region, when he said in an interview
with Sankei Shimbun that Japan should consider nuclear weapons and
"start discussing whether to seek a nuclear sharing arrangement similar
to the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation's nuclear deterrence policy".

Of course, Abe knows that [acquiring nuclear weapons in Japan would be
**crossing the line**, if not unthinkable]{.underline}; but he is also
aware that threats to his country are real. As he explained, "Japan's
geopolitical situation shares points in common with Ukraine's... If
Ukraine were a member of the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation
\[NATO\]... Russia would not have invaded."

Indeed, what happened to Ukraine came as a shock, not only to Japan, but
also to other countries, particularly those dependent on others to meet
their security needs. Abe is also aware of the Japanese public's
concerns about the ongoing North Korea threat and China's increasing
aggression in East Asia and the South China Sea region.

Of course, [Japan has **all the capabilities** to be a nuclear power and
already is in possession of 45.5 tonnes of reparated Plutonium, enough
to **produce 6,ooo nuclear bombs**. Japan could go nuclear within six to
12 months; nevertheless, Japan **will not acquire nuclear weapons for
several reasons**]{.underline}.

**[First]{.underline}**, as is well known, [Japan is the only country to
have suffered from nuclear attacks]{.underline}. This was the main
reason for its surrender in the Second World War. After the war, Japan
adopted the [**Three Non-Nuclear Principles**, which stipulate
non-**possession, non-production, and non-introduction of nuclear
weapons in its territories**. Japan is still **politically firmly
stuck** to these principles in accordance with its Peace
Constitution]{.underline}. Former prime minister, Yoshihide Suga, vowed
not only to prevent the proliferation of nuclear weapons, but also to
free the world of all nuclear arms. This [Japanese sensitivity to
nuclear technology was further reinforced after the **Fukushima
disaster** in 2011]{.underline}.

**[Second]{.underline}**, The [**anti-nuclear sentiment is strong**
among Japanese people]{.underline}. Indeed, the [Japanese are strikingly
opposed to nuclear weapons]{.underline}. According to a prevous public
opinion poll, [**82 per cent** of Japanese people are against nuclear
weapons, while 69 per cent are also opposed to having these weapons
brought into their country's territories]{.underline}.

**[Third]{.underline}**, it is not only the Japanese public that is
opposed to nuclear weapons. Indeed, there [many **internal players who
reject** these nuclear arms]{.underline}. The nuc[lear energy industry
in Japan is **advanced and thriving**, but this only gained acceptance
on account of the '**nuclear energy, not nuclear weapons'** deal of the
1950s]{.underline}. Thus [ministries, regulatory bodies, industrial
groups, and local governments have the **right of veto** against any
political decision in this regard]{.underline}. These entities rely on
strong public opposition, of course.

Another important point here is that the [nuclear facilities in Japan
**operate in compliance** with several multilateral agreements and
treaties]{.underline}. It is also noteworthy that [all Japanese nuclear
activities are **under the regulatory control** of the International
Atomic Energy Agency]{.underline}.

**[Forth]{.underline}**, [Japan]{.underline} is still, and [will remain,
under the American nuclear umbrella]{.underline}. [All American
administrations, **including Trump's**, have **reaffirmed their
commitment** to Japan's security]{.underline}. Therefore, [US **nuclear
capacities** together with its conventional military capabilities in the
region are **sufficient** to deter any North Korean security threat to
Japan, including a nuclear attack]{.underline}.

However, [US **withdrawal**]{.underline} from the Pacific, though not
yet expected, could invoke Japan to conclude that the "nuclear option"
might be the best choice. Indeed, if the US withdraws its security
commitment to Japan, which includes [the **nuclear umbrella**, Japan
could seriously consider becoming a nuclear power. But this is **not
expected**]{.underline}, at least not in the near future.

**[Fifth]{.underline}**, despite Japan's potential to develop a nuclear
capacity, it still faces [significant challenges in the acquisition of
nuclear weapons]{.underline}. The issue is [**not limited to making** an
atomic bomb. Developing a nuclear deterrent will be no easy matter for
Japan. It will need to conduct a series of nuclear **tests, over a
period of years**, to achieve reliable nuclear
capabilities]{.underline}.

This also raises another significant question: **[How will Japan deploy
such weapons]{.underline}**? Japan [**does not have** nuclear-capable
aircraft, nor does it have specialised bombers or strike
planes]{.underline}. The Japan Air Self-Defence Force does have several
US warplanes, such as the [**F-15,** which can be modified to carry
nuclear bombs. However, deploying these would **require US approval** to
access the "black boxes" on the plane, that is the electronic codes. It
is hard to imagine that the **US would consent** to this]{.underline}.

Despite the ongoing debates as to whether Japan should change its
long-standing policy of nuclear abstention, given the profound changes
in the regional and international security environment, [it is **not
expected that** Japan will opt for nuclearisation, at least not in the
foreseeable future]{.underline}. However, the debate on sharing nuclear
power with the US will remain active from some time.

### No Prolif---Japan---China Threat

#### Threat of Chinese retaliation means Japan never proliferates

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional
Studies from the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies; Amy
Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of
Washington; Roger Clemens, B. A. in Political Economy and Global and
Regional Studies from the University of Washington; Claire Paiemont, B.
A. in International Studies and Economies from the University of
Washington; Kay Reed, B. A. in Global and Regional Studies from the
University of Washington; The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program @
the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies @ University of
Washington, \"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation\",
2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

Despite [China's rising military and economic capability]{.underline},
its ascension and regional hegemony [**does not pose** the existential
threat needed to warrant Japanese nuclear proliferation]{.underline}.
[Pursuing nuclear weapons would only **serve to incense**
China]{.underline}, **[escalate regional tensions]{.underline}**, and
diminish Japanese security. Japan and China have a long history of
violent conflict and their relations have only recently been stabilized.
From 2017 to 2019, and again in February 2023, [Japan and China have had
several in-person meetings to discuss prominent issues and future
cooperation]{.underline} in an attempt to resume regular talks and
alleviate tension (Ming 2021; Murakami 2023). The February meeting
covered China's relationship with Russia, Japan's military expansion and
the Cross-Strait conflict. While none of these meetings with China have
yielded any resolution to their outstanding problems, [it appears both
states **understand the value of de-escalation**]{.underline}.
Nonetheless, rising nationalist sentiment and the legacy of their
historical antagonism continues to hamper progress. Thus, [considering
the fragile nature of Japan-China relations and the prospects for
improvement, Japan should not proliferate to **maintain progress with
China** and to prevent endangering the Japanese public]{.underline}.

Moreover, despite their historical and contemporary grievances, [Japan
and China have been core trade partners for decades]{.underline}, and
their [**economic** **interdependence** continues to grow]{.underline}.
[Both states are major mutual exporters and importers and any
substantial reduction in trade would be **extremely costly** to both,
but especially Japan]{.underline}. [Japanese nuclear proliferation would
draw China's ire and move it to fervent **retaliation**]{.underline},
which would likely [include **severe military** and economic
**measures** to punish Japan]{.underline}. The Japanese pursuit of
nuclear weapons would likely commit irreparable damage to their economic
relationship. Therefore, Japan should not proliferate in order to
maintain its fruitful economic relationship with China.

#### [Interdependence]{.underline} and [other security measures]{.underline}. 

John T. **Deacon &** Etel **Solingen 23**, PhD, Political Science at the

Dilemmas in a Challenging New Era,\" East Asia Forum, 06/01/2023,

The [durability of Japan's commitment to]{.underline}
**[abiding]{.underline}** [by the]{.underline} **[NPT]{.underline}**
[raises an important consideration]{.underline}. [Some]{.underline}
security [analysts]{.underline} have **[predicted]{.underline}**
[Japan]{.underline} would [seek its]{.underline} **[own nuclear
deterrent]{.underline}** in tandem with three of its neighbours'
nuclearisation. Yet [Japan's]{.underline} **[decades-old nuclear
abstention]{.underline}** [defies those predictions, which
neglected]{.underline} **[other]{.underline}**
[considerations]{.underline}.

Early in the Cold War, [Japan's commitment to]{.underline} global
**[economic interdependence]{.underline}** [prioritised stability and
global market access]{.underline}. [This]{.underline}
**[shaped]{.underline}** [incentives to]{.underline}
**[remain]{.underline}** [a non-nuclear weapons state and]{.underline}
**[reduce risks]{.underline}** to its economy. Japan also capped defence
spending at 1 per cent of GDP. While by 2020 China had surpassed the
United States as Japan's top export market, Japan still relied on US
extended deterrence despite its own technological capabilities.

Unlike in South Korea, [Japanese public opinion remains]{.underline}
**[opposed]{.underline}** [to nuclear weapons acquisition]{.underline}.
[A]{.underline} **[2019]{.underline}** national **[survey]{.underline}**
[found 75 per cent of respondents supported]{.underline} ratifying
[the]{.underline} **[T]{.underline}**reaty on the
**[P]{.underline}**rohibition of **[N]{.underline}**uclear
**[W]{.underline}**eapons.

[To]{.underline} **[reassure the public]{.underline}**, [Japan's leaders
launched]{.underline} the largest [**mil**itary
**expansion**]{.underline} since 1945. While Article 9 of the
Constitution famously renounced the right to maintain military forces,
in 2015 the Diet voted to allow Japanese forces to deploy overseas to
defend allies. In 2017, the 1 per cent of GDP cap in [defence
expenditures was]{.underline} **[superseded]{.underline}**,
[and]{.underline} in 2022 Kishida raised it to 2 per cent by 2027 --- on
track to become the third-largest defence budget globally. **[Joint
military drills]{.underline}** [have increased, and]{.underline} Japan
has [signed]{.underline} **[new defence agreements]{.underline}**
[with]{.underline} **[Australia]{.underline}** [and the]{.underline}
**[U]{.underline}**nited **[K]{.underline}**ingdom.

#### Acquiring nukes is too costly for Japan

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional
Studies from the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies; Amy
Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of
Washington; Roger Clemens, B. A. in Political Economy and Global and
Regional Studies from the University of Washington; Claire Paiemont, B.
A. in International Studies and Economies from the University of
Washington; Kay Reed, B. A. in Global and Regional Studies from the
University of Washington; The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program @
the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies @ University of
Washington, \"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation\",
2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

Domestic Costs of Proliferation

The [construction of a single nuclear warhead can **cost billions** of
dollars]{.underline}, and the [costs of **maintaining** a nuclear
arsenal would be **even higher**]{.underline}. [For Japan, the costs of
building nuclear weapons would be especially significant given its
**limited resources**]{.underline}. To give a comparable estimate, a
South Korean government analysis estimated that it cost North Korea
between \$1.1 billion and \$3.2 billion to develop its nuclear weapons
program (Pearson and Park 2016). However, accurate estimates are
difficult to obtain given the secrecy surrounding the program, and the
exact figures for Japan would be determined by the size of the project.
To provide an approximating estimate, it is possible to estimate Japan's
potential nuclear stockpile through a comparison in American and
Japanese military expenditures. For the fiscal year of 2023, the U.S.
Department of Defense (DoD) has allocated \$773 billion, and the JSDF
has allocated approximately \$52 billion (U.S. Department of Defense
2022; Tian & Liang 2023). Utilizing the two budgets as a proportion
(6.7%) of military sizes and the most recent estimated size of the U.S.
nuclear arsenal (5,428), Japan would likely end up compiling 300--400
nuclear warheads in its arsenal (Kristensen & Korda 2022). [If Japan
were to utilize]{.underline} the modernized W87-1 [nuclear
warheads]{.underline} (\$14.8 billion per warhead as of 2020) for their
arsenal, it could [cost anywhere from \$4.4 trillion to \$**5.9
trillion**]{.underline} (U.S. Government Accountability Office 2020).
[Even if Japan were to confine the size of its arsenal to]{.underline}
that of the DPRK ([20 warheads]{.underline}), it would still [cost
approximately \$**300 billion**]{.underline} (U.S. Government
Accountability Office 2020; Kristensen & Korda 2022). To account for
these costs, there would need to be a significant increase in Japanese
defense spending in Japan, which was already increased from 1% to 2% by
the Kishida administration in 2022 (Kajimoto and Yamaguchi 2022).

In addition to the direct costs of building and maintaining a nuclear
weapons program, there are [indirect costs associated with increased
defense spending]{.underline}. Given that military spending directly
diminishes the spending power of the government, [spending a greater
amount of money on defense would likely cause a **decreased**
availability of **funding** **for** **other** important government
**infrastructure** and social welfare projects]{.underline}.

This includes the opportunity cost of developing a nuclear weapons
program instead of allocating resources to sustainable nuclear energy.
The [cost of establishing a nuclear weapons regime would be **twofold**:
the implicit cost of building new facilities, and the opportunity cost
of the **energy** **capacity** **lost** due to the transfer to the
procurement of nuclear weapons]{.underline}. Developing nuclear energy
has been a priority of Japan's for a long time --- given its limited
natural resources and high energy needs. However, investing in nuclear
weapons would divert resources from the development of sustainable
energy solutions. As sustainable energy becomes an international
priority to slow the effects of climate change, it is important that
Japan continue to devote resources to the nuclear energy sector to
further the domestic and global initiative for sustainable energy
practice, and to divert these resources to a nuclear weapons program
would significantly hinder Japan's ability to accomplish those
objectives.

Therefore, the [estimated direct and opportunity costs of proliferation
are **too** **costly** for Japan given the other significant
consequences of proliferating]{.underline}. Serving as a hindrance to
other federal programming, a Japanese nuclear weapons program would
significantly detract from its current nuclear energy program and the
overall national energy supply.

### No Prolif---Japan---Trade 

#### Sanctions, tariffs, and embargoes means it's impossible to prolif

**Acevedo et al. 23** \[written under the advisory of Robert J.
Pekkanen, Ph.D. Professor @ the Henry M. Jackson School of International
Studies; Mike M. Mochizuki, Ph.D. Professor @ the Elliott School of
International Affairs; Ethan Acevedo, B. A. in Global and Regional
Studies from the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies; Amy
Bishop-Smith, B. A. in International Relations from the University of
Washington; Roger Clemens, B. A. in Political Economy and Global and
Regional Studies from the University of Washington; Claire Paiemont, B.
A. in International Studies and Economies from the University of
Washington; Kay Reed, B. A. in Global and Regional Studies from the
University of Washington; The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program @
the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies @ University of
Washington, \"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation\",
2023, Chapter III: Attendant Consequences of Nuclear Proliferation, p.
49-50, accessed 7-16-2025\] /ety

\*\*CPTPP = Comprehensive and Progressive Agreement for Trans-Pacific
Partnership

The Effect on Trade

The impact of trade sanctions can be severe, especially on countries
that rely heavily on imports as is the case with Japan. [If Japan were
to pursue a nuclear weapons program, it would face **serious**
**economic** **consequences** from its trade partners, which has the
potential to be devastating for Japan\'s economy]{.underline}. Trade
sanctions such as [**tariffs**, **quotas**, **and** **embargoes** would
lead to increased costs for Japanese businesses]{.underline}, resulting
in increased prices for Japanese consumers. Consequently, a decline in
purchasing power and a reduction in overall consumer spending would be
likely, which, in turn, could cause a decrease in economic growth and an
increase in unemployment. Export revenue would decrease, and imports
would be significantly more expensive --- if accessible at all in the
case of embargos. The Japanese current account balance would deteriorate
resulting in a loss of the account surplus it has dedicated itself to
maintaining for years. This would be particularly damaging to the
Japanese economy, where the country's account surplus has been a symbol
of "export might" and a "source of confidence in the safe-haven yen"
(Kajimoto 2023).

Similarly, [Japanese proliferation would likely jeopardize existing
trade agreements]{.underline}. The Comprehensive and Progressive
Agreement for Trans-Pacific Partnership (CPTPP) and the Association of
Southeast Asian Nations (ASEAN) are key trade partnerships for Japan
that might otherwise be negatively impacted by proliferation. The CPTPP,
a trade agreement between 11 countries in the Asia-Pacific region, aims
to boost economic integration and growth. Japan\'s membership in the
agreement provides the country with access to a large and dynamic
market, and serves as a strategic tool to counterbalance China\'s
influence in the region. In November of 2019, Japan exported just over
800 million yen worth of goods to CPTPP member countries (Jaramillo
2020).

Similarly, Japan\'s partnership with ASEAN, a regional intergovernmental
organization, aims to strengthen economic ties and promote peace and
stability in Southeast Asia. Japan\'s participation in the CPTPP and
ASEAN has significant implications for the country\'s economy. The
agreements provide Japan with opportunities to promote its businesses,
technology, and culture in the region. Japan\'s membership in the CPTPP,
in particular, has the potential to stimulate economic growth and
innovation by lowering tariffs, harmonizing regulations, and
facilitating the flow of goods and services. The [CPTPP eliminates an
estimated 95% of tariffs for trade of goods between CPTPP
countries]{.underline}, which contributes to reduced business costs
allowing consumers to benefit from lower prices. (US-ASEAN 2021).

If [Japan were to adopt nuclear weapons]{.underline} this could [result
in their **removal** from the treatie]{.underline}s, or alternatively,
the [treaty members might form a new agreement without
Japan]{.underline}. Thus, Japan would no longer benefit from lower
tariffs and regulations. This would increase transaction and business
costs, stunting economic growth. Japan would lose access to important
regional markets and its exported goods would become less competitive.

The deterioration of trade relationships would also have a negative
effect on the Japanese energy industry. As mentioned in Chapter I
Section V of the report, [Japan is **heavily reliant** on imported
oil]{.underline}, particularly from Russia, who provided 8% of Japan\'s
oil imports in 2020 (U.S. Energy Information Administration 2020).
Japan\'s [dependence on imported oil is primarily due to a **lack of
domestic natural resources** with only a few small oil fields in the
country]{.underline}. If Japan were to pursue a nuclear weapons program,
it would [jeopardize its relationship with key trade partners such as
Russia]{.underline}. This could [cause Russia to implement export
**quotas** that would make it difficult for Japan to import the oil it
needs]{.underline}. Oil imports are critical inputs for many sectors of
the Japanese economy including transportation, industry, and
agriculture. According to the Japan Oil, Gas and Metals National
Corporation (JOGMEC), [crude oil and natural gas together account for
more than **60% of Japan\'s primary energy consumption**]{.underline}
(JOGMEC 2021). [Therefore, any disruption in this import supply chain
could have serious consequences]{.underline} for the country\'s economic
well-being. The [price of oil in Japan would sky rocket, generating
inflation]{.underline}. Worse still, shortages of oil caused by
embargoes could [lead to an **energy** **crisis**]{.underline}. Due to
the Japanese reliance on imported oil, trade sanctions and embargoes on
Japan would destabilize the Japanese energy supply and likely cause an
energy crisis. This was exemplified by the Arab oil embargo of 1973 ---
during which Arab countries implemented oil export restrictions on
pro-Israeli governments, causing oil prices to jump from \$2 per barrel
to \$11 per barrel (Schneider, n.d.). This caused an economic recession
in Japan, marking the end of a period of rapid growth in the Japanese
economy. Thus, it is clear that [trade sanctions on oil imports to Japan
could have **disastrous** **effects** on the Japanese economy, and
Japanese proliferation would likely cause such sanctions]{.underline} to
occur due to the international backlash inherent in backing out of the
Non-Proliferation Treaty and obtaining nuclear weapons.

**[possessing]{.underline}** their own [**nucl**ear

weapons]{.underline} **[remains firm]{.underline}** [among]{.underline}
Japanese [citizens including]{.underline} **[academic]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} **[scientific]{.underline}** [circles]{.underline}.
Except for right-wing Japanese politicians, [the]{.underline}
**[majority]{.underline}** [of Japanese lawmakers are also not in favor
of Japan's nuclearization]{.underline}. Thus, [they are]{.underline}
**[not willing]{.underline}** [to change the]{.underline} so-called
